40 — Vendor ecosystem & taxonomy
50+ vendor types mapped — the complete event industry ecosystem
A serious full-service Plnr touches 25–40 distinct vendor types for a standard wedding and 40–60+ for luxury, cultural, or corporate events. This section documents the complete vendor ecosystem, identifies the structural overlap zones where clients get double-charged, and explains the role-based taxonomy decision that replaces the old flat vendor type list in §3. This research drives the platform’s category architecture and Cue’s overlap detection logic.

Complete vendor ecosystem — 10 groups, 50+ types

GroupVendor typesTypical count per event
Core venue & infrastructureVenue, catering company, bar service / alcohol provider, equipment rentals (tables, chairs, linens), specialty rentals (lounge furniture), AV/production, power/generators (outdoor), restroom trailers (outdoor luxury)5–8 types
Food & beverage expansionCake designer/bakery, dessert vendors (gelato, donuts), coffee cart/espresso bar, late-night snack vendors, food trucks, specialty cuisine vendors (ethnic/cultural), sommeliers/wine curators, mixologists2–5 types
Design & decorEvent designer/stylist, florist, decor rental company, lighting designer, draping specialist, stage/set builder, signage & stationery, balloon artists, dance floor wraps/custom flooring, tabletop rentals (plates, chargers, flatware), ice sculptures/specialty installations3–8 types
EntertainmentDJ, live band, ceremony musicians (string quartet, harpist, pianist), specialty performers (sax, violin), MC/host, cultural performers (dancers, drummers, mariachi), photo booth, interactive entertainment (casino tables, lawn games), speakers (corporate)2–6 types
Media & contentPhotographer, videographer, content creator (social media — rapidly growing category), drone operator, live streaming team2–4 types
Personal & beauty servicesHair stylist, makeup artist, wardrobe stylist, tailor/alterations, personal concierge, bridal assistant, spa/wellness providers2–5 types
Transportation & logisticsGuest transportation (buses, shuttles), luxury car service, valet company, parking management, travel coordinator (destination events)1–4 types
Guest experienceHotel room block coordinators, welcome gift companies, invitation/RSVP management, guest apps/websites, concierge services, childcare providers1–4 types
Planning & coordination layer Full-service planner — strategy, vendor selection, design coordination, and day-of execution. 150–400 hrs per event. Fee: $4K–$15K+ or 12–25% of total budget. The Orgnz hands over everything.

Partial / month-of planner — inherits existing vendor contracts, builds final timeline, runs day-of. Comes in 4–8 weeks out. Fee: $2K–$8K. Serves the “I did most of it but I’m scared of the finish line” client.

Day-of coordinator — execution only, no prior involvement. Shows up and runs the room. Fee: $800–$3,500. Completely different value proposition from full-service — same Plnr portal, very different sub-role tag and profile presentation.

Event producer / production manager — technical execution specialist. AV systems, staging, lighting crew, run-of-show calling, speaker management, hybrid/livestream production. Corporate events primarily — often hired alongside a Plnr, not instead of one. Currently absent from Plnr portal spec. Must be added before Corporate Tier launch.

Stage manager / show caller — builds and calls the cue sheet on event day. Directs crew, manages technical transitions, owns minute-by-minute run-of-show execution. Live events, corporate galas, multi-day conferences. Distinct from production manager (who designs the technical spec; stage manager executes it). 		1–3 types per event — Plnr portal handles all five sub-roles via profile tags. Corporate events often need Plnr + producer simultaneously.
Compliance & specialtyInsurance providers, security personnel, permitting specialists, health/safety compliance, fire marshal coordination (large events), restroom trailers1–4 types

Planning & coordination layer — 5 distinct sub-roles

The original pasted research listed four types in the planning layer. Research confirms five distinct sub-roles that the Plnr portal must support — each with different tooling needs, different pricing models, and different event type fits. A Plnr on EvntCue registers their sub-role(s) on their profile. Cue’s recommendation gate (§42) matches the right sub-role to each Orgnz’s event type and budget.
Sub-roleWhat they doTooling needed in Plnr portalTypical feeEvent fit
Full-service planner Strategy + vendor selection + design + day-of execution. Involved from first conversation to final payment. 150–400 hours per event. Full proposal builder, mood board, vendor matching, commission ledger, service fee manager, run-of-show generator, BEO access, post-event ROI report $4,000–$15,000+ flat fee, or 12–25% of total event budget. Luxury market: $15,000–$50,000+ Weddings $30K+, luxury events, corporate galas, destination events, any event with 15+ vendor categories
Partial / month-of planner Inherits existing vendor contracts 4–8 weeks before the event. Builds final timeline, confirms all vendors, runs day-of. The “finish line” hire for Orgnzs who started planning themselves. Contract import tool, vendor confirmation tracker, timeline builder, run-of-show generator, day-of checklist. Does not need full vendor matching or mood board. $2,000–$8,000 flat fee Weddings $15K–$30K, social events where Orgnz has done most planning, anyone who “got too far in” without professional help
Day-of coordinator Execution only. No prior involvement. Shows up day-of with the plan the Orgnz already built, manages vendor arrival windows, timeline, and logistics. Zero pre-event planning. Run-of-show viewer, vendor contact sheet, day-of checklist, offline bundle. No proposal builder, no commission ledger, no vendor matching — those belong to full-service. $800–$3,500 flat fee Small events under $15K, experienced Orgnzs who planned everything themselves, church weddings with on-site venue coordinator
Event producer / production manager Technical execution specialist. AV systems, staging, lighting crew management, load-in/load-out scheduling, run-of-show calling. While the Plnr designs the vision, the producer makes it work technically. Corporate events almost always need both. Small social events: one person often does both. Technical rider builder, AV spec sheet, minute-by-minute run-of-show (show caller format), load-in schedule, crew management, equipment inventory. These tools do not exist in the current Plnr portal spec — required before Corporate Tier launch. $3,000–$15,000+ for corporate events. Often hired as part of an AV/production company rather than independently. Corporate events of any size, multi-day conferences, galas with complex staging, live entertainment events, hybrid/virtual events. Required alongside a Plnr for events over ~300 guests with AV complexity.
Stage manager / show caller Show-calling specialist. Directs the production team in real time from a cue sheet — tells the lighting operator when to change, the band when to start, the AV team when to roll video. Distinct from the production manager (who sets up the technical systems) — the stage manager runs them during the show. Cue sheet builder, real-time show calling interface (mobile-optimized), speaker/performer briefing sheets. New tooling required — does not exist in current spec. $500–$3,000 per event, often included in production company package Corporate galas, live entertainment events, award ceremonies, multi-act entertainment, any event with complex timing requiring a single person calling cues in real time
Two gaps requiring new Plnr portal tooling before Corporate Tier launch: (1) Technical rider builder — structured input for AV specs, staging dimensions, power requirements, load-in windows. Currently no equivalent exists in the spec. (2) Cue sheet / show caller interface — mobile-optimized, real-time show calling view where the stage manager sees the run-of-show as a live cue list they step through during the event. Both tools use data already in the system (run-of-show, vendor assignments, timeline) but present it in a production-facing format rather than a planning-facing format.

Additional missing categories — event type and edge case gaps

GapWhy it mattersEvntCue action
Event designer / creative director Distinct from Plnr — the designer develops the full aesthetic vision (color palette, decor concept, floral direction, overall look) and hands it to the execution team. Many Plnrs also design; many designers also plan. The industry confuses them constantly, and Orgnzs hire both thinking they’re different when one person can do both, or hire neither when they think one covers the other. Design services tagged as a sub-role on the Plnr profile (not a separate portal). When Orgnz books a Plnr with design tagged, Cue asks: “Your Plnr also offers event design — do you still want to book a separate event designer?” This is overlap zone 2 from §26 overlap detection.
Nonprofit / fundraiser event type A distinct Orgnz use case with genuinely different needs: sponsorship tracking, donor management, volunteer coordination, strict budget accountability, and mission alignment in every vendor decision. The §26 event type library has no nonprofit/fundraiser template. A Plnr specializing in nonprofit events has a completely different toolkit than a wedding Plnr. Add “Nonprofit / Fundraiser” to the §26 event type template library. Vendor categories: Venue, Catr, AV/Tech, Entertainment, Photographer, Signage, Sponsor management. Auto-populated documents: donation tracking sheet, sponsor tier table, volunteer schedule, auction item management. New Plnr sub-tag: Nonprofit specialist.
Destination event edge case A destination event breaks the geographic proximity assumption baked into the §26 vendor suggestion engine (currently weights proximity at 15%). If the event is in a different city or country from the Orgnz’s home location, Cue’s matching algorithm would weight local vendors — vendors who are in the wrong geography entirely. When event address differs from Orgnz home location by more than a configurable radius (default: 50 miles), Cue switches vendor matching to event-location-based proximity, not Orgnz-location-based. Also surfaces a travel coordinator vendor type automatically. Flag on event record: is_destination_event = true. Admin-configurable radius threshold.

The 7 structural overlap zones

These are the zones where clients most commonly pay twice for the same function. They exist because the industry evolved with vendors selling point services, not coordinated packages. EvntCue’s role-based taxonomy collapses the most dangerous overlaps into single categories, and Cue’s overlap detection (see §26) surfaces warnings for the rest.
#Overlap zoneHow it happensEvntCue solution
1Catering vs. Rentals vs. VenueVenue includes tables/chairs. Caterer also quotes rentals. Rental company also quotes same items. Client gets three bids for one need.Role category “Décor & Rentals” is distinct from venue inclusions. Cue reads venue contract fields and warns: “Your venue includes tables and chairs for [N] guests.”
2Planner vs. DesignerMany Plnrs offer design. Many designers offer planning. Orgnzs hire both thinking they’re different roles.EvntCue Plnr portal is logistics + execution. Design is a tagged service within the Plnr’s profile. Cue asks: “Your Plnr offers design services — do you still need a separate event designer?”
3DJ vs. AV CompanyDJ brings sound equipment. AV company also quotes sound. Orgnz pays for audio twice.Collapsed into “Audio & Music Experience” category. Cue warns when both are booked for same event.
4Florist vs. Décor CompanyFlorist provides centerpieces. Decor company quotes centerpieces too. Overlapping inventory, duplicated cost.Separate categories (Florals & Design vs. Décor & Rentals) with clear scope definitions. Cue flags centerpiece scope when both are booked.
5Photographer vs. Content CreatorBoth booked. Neither confirmed who’s doing social clips. Deliverables undefined, budgets duplicated.Kept as one category (Photography & Media) but with distinct sub-role tags. Cue sends a scope-confirmation prompt when both sub-roles are booked.
6Venue vs. Planner (the big one)Client thinks venue coordinator = Plnr. Skips hiring a Plnr. Arrives on event day with no one running the room.Cue educates, not warns: “Venue coordinators manage the space. A Plnr manages your entire event — vendors, timeline, everything else.” No warning because both should be hired.
7Transportation vs. Venue LogisticsVenue includes valet. Plnr also books external valet company. Both charge, only one needed.Cue reads venue contract valet field. If populated, warns before Transportation & Logistics valet is confirmed.

Why role-based categories beat vendor type lists

Type-based (old model)
DJ · AV company · Lighting designer · Sound engineer. Four separate search categories, four separate budget line items, four separate commission rate rows. Client has no way to know which ones overlap. Platform takes commission on all four even when two are duplicates.
Role-based (v16.0 model)
Audio & Music Experience covers DJ + ceremony musicians + AV sound. Visual Production & Lighting covers lighting designer + AV screens + stage builder. Two categories, zero double-charge risk, cleaner commission structure, faster Orgnz decision-making.
Platform benefit
Fewer categories = faster booking flow = higher conversion. Cue’s matching algorithm runs on roles, not types — better results with less complexity. Commission rate table is more defensible to vendors when they understand the category logic.

Strategic implications for EvntCue

The research behind this taxonomy confirms four platform decisions:
1. Catr hard definition holds
The Food & Beverage Expansion group (cake, dessert vendors, coffee carts, food trucks, sommeliers, mixologists) confirms that the Catr portal definition — any person or business providing food or beverage consumed at the event — is exactly right. All of these types route through Catr. No exceptions.
2. Commission stacking is the real problem
The overlap zones are not accidental — they’re structural to the industry. On a $300K gala with 60 vendor types, commission stacking without visibility could cost the Orgnz tens of thousands. EvntCue’s commission intelligence (§8) is not a nice-to-have — it is the solution to the industry’s most expensive chronic problem.
3. “Roles not vendors” is a UX principle
Building the event dashboard around roles (“Audio & Music Experience” category with a status bar) rather than types (“DJ,” “Band,” “AV” as separate rows) reduces cognitive load for Orgnzs, reduces over-booking risk, and makes the Plnr’s coordination job more legible. The Plnr sees one slot to fill, one confirmation to get.
4. Content creator is a new first-class category
The research identifies content creators (real-time social media coverage) as a rapidly growing vendor type that is “increasingly redundant if not positioned correctly” relative to photographers. EvntCue should support both as sub-roles within Photography & Media, with Cue asking scope-confirmation questions when both are booked. This is a 2024–2025 trend that older platforms haven’t structured for.
5. The Plnr portal needs two tiers before Corporate Tier launches
The current Plnr portal spec is built for social event planners. Corporate events require event producers and stage managers — roles with distinct tooling needs: technical riders, AV spec sheets, minute-by-minute run-of-show, hybrid/livestream coordination, and speaker management. Before the Corporate Tier launches (Phase 2), the Plnr portal must support producer and stage manager sub-roles with the appropriate dashboard modules. A corporate Orgnz hiring only through the current Plnr portal would get a wedding-brained spec sheet and no technical production tooling.
6. Nonprofit and destination are missing event types
Nonprofit fundraisers have genuinely different needs: sponsor table management, donation tracking, silent auction logistics, volunteer coordination, strict budget ceiling with sponsor offset. Destination events break the geographic proximity assumption in the vendor matching engine. Both are now in the §26 event type library as of v17.0. Destination mode switches Cue’s matching algorithm from proximity-weighted to travel-willing-gated. Nonprofit mode surfaces budget ceiling warnings and sponsor acknowledgment tooling.

42 — Plnr recommendation gate
Budget-triggered Cue intervention before the first vendor category unlocks
After an Orgnz sets their event budget and before the first vendor category card unlocks, Cue runs a single decision: does this event need a Plnr? If yes, Cue surfaces a recommendation — not a sales pitch, a piece of honest advice. The Orgnz always has the final choice. Their answer determines Cue’s behavior for the entire rest of the event.
Why this gate exists: Three reasons. (1) Plnr revenue protection — a $60K event that books without a Plnr is a lost commission stream for the entire Plnr tier. (2) Plnr acquisition channel — Cue becomes an active referral engine at the exact moment the Orgnz is most receptive, right after seeing their budget. (3) Orgnz expectation setting — an Orgnz who proceeds without a Plnr after seeing the suggestion has explicitly chosen to self-manage. Cue stays in decision-engine mode and treats them as an experienced Orgnz, not a hand-holding case.

When the gate fires

TriggerConditionNotes
Budget setevents.budget_cents is confirmed by OrgnzGate fires once, on budget confirmation. If the Orgnz changes their budget later, Cue re-evaluates and resurfaces the gate if the new budget crosses a tier boundary.
Venue confirmed OR bypassedVenue gate (§27) has been resolved — venue either confirmed or private venue bypass usedGate fires only after venue is resolved. Event date and location are needed to match the right Plnr sub-role and geographic coverage.
No Plnr already assignedevents.plnr_tenant_id IS NULLIf the Orgnz arrived with a Plnr already in mind (common for referrals), the gate is skipped. Cue goes straight to companion mode.
First vendor category not yet unlockedNo bookings record exists for this event yetGate fires before any booking, not after. Catching the Orgnz before the first vendor inquiry is the whole point.

The gate — four Cue responses by budget tier

Under $10K — Cue manages directly
“Your budget is $8,500. That’s a solid foundation for a great event — I can help you book everything directly. Ready to get started?”
No Plnr suggestion. Cue stays in decision-engine mode. Orgnz goes straight to vendor matching. At this budget, a Plnr fee would represent 10–35% of the total budget — a disproportionate spend.
$10K–$20K — Day-of coordinator suggestion
“At $14,000, most people plan this themselves and hire a day-of coordinator to run the actual event. Want me to show you a few who cover [event location]? Or I can help you start booking vendors directly — your call.”
Soft suggestion. Two options offered with no pressure. Selecting “Show me coordinators” opens a filtered Plnr search scoped to day-of sub-role only. Selecting “Book directly” dismisses the gate and unlocks vendor categories immediately.
$20K–$40K — Partial planner suggestion
“Your budget is $28,000 with an estimated 14 vendor categories. Events at this scale typically benefit from a planner helping in the final stretch. I can match you with a few partial planners who specialize in [event type] — or you can manage everything yourself. Which works better for you?”
Stronger suggestion with specific reasoning (vendor category count, event type). Cue surfaces the complexity data that justifies the recommendation so it feels informational, not pushy. Orgnz still has two clear paths, no pressure.
$40K+ — Full-service planner suggestion
“At $55,000 with 20+ vendor categories, this is the kind of event where a full-service planner earns their fee many times over — in time saved, vendor relationships, and things that just don’t go wrong. Here are three planners who match your event type and budget range. Want to review them before we start booking vendors?”
Confident recommendation. Cue explains the value proposition directly. Three matched Plnrs are surfaced immediately so the recommendation feels concrete, not abstract. The “before we start booking vendors” framing positions it as a sequencing decision, not a hard block. Orgnz can still dismiss and proceed solo.

Corporate and destination overrides

Event typeOverride conditionCue response
Corporate eventevents.event_type IN ('corporate_gala', 'conference', 'corporate_meeting', 'product_launch') regardless of budget“Corporate events almost always benefit from both a planner and a production manager — the planner handles vendor coordination, the producer handles the technical execution. Want me to find both, or just one? You can also proceed directly if your team is handling production internally.”
Destination eventevents.is_destination_event = true (set when event address > 50 miles from Orgnz home location)“Destination events have a lot of moving parts that are harder to manage remotely — vendor relationships you don’t have locally, logistics you can’t walk over and check. I can research local vendors, permits, and logistics for [event city], but a destination-experienced Plnr with boots on the ground is the real unlock. Want to see options, or start with what I can pull together on [event city]?” — Cue destination intelligence module activates (see §44 Co-Plnr for local partner network).

What happens after the gate

Orgnz choicePlatform actionCue mode
Accepts Plnr matchPlnr invitation sent. On Plnr acceptance, events.plnr_tenant_id is set. Vendor categories remain locked until Plnr accepts. Orgnz dashboard switches to the 4-zone Plnr-assigned view (§25).Companion mode — educational, no vendor decisions surfaced to Orgnz
Declines — books directlyGate dismissed. All vendor categories unlock. Orgnz dashboard shows full vendor matching interface. events.plnr_recommended = true, events.plnr_declined = true logged for analytics.Decision-engine mode — full vendor matching, budget intelligence, active suggestions
Dismisses — decides laterGate is snoozed. Vendor categories unlock. After 48 hours or on the second login, Cue resurfaces the recommendation once more. After second dismissal, gate is permanently dismissed for this event.Decision-engine mode until Plnr is assigned

Plnr matching logic inside the gate

When the gate surfaces a Plnr recommendation, it shows up to 3 matched Plnrs. The matching algorithm for the gate differs from the general vendor suggestion engine — it weights Plnr-specific factors.
FactorWeightData source
Sub-role match (full-service, partial, day-of, producer)Hard gate — must match the suggested tierPlnr profile sub-role tags
Event type specialization35%Plnr profile event type tags (wedding, corporate, nonprofit, destination, etc.)
Geographic coverage25%Plnr service area vs. event location; destination specialists flagged separately
Budget fit20%Plnr fee range vs. Orgnz budget (target: Plnr fee = 10–20% of total budget)
Platform performance score15%Plnr composite score from review ratings, on-time rate, Orgnz satisfaction surveys
AvailabilityHard gate — must have event date openPlnr calendar

Admin controls

Threshold configuration
All four budget tier thresholds are admin-configurable in the EvntCue admin panel. Default values: $10K / $20K / $40K. Admin can adjust as platform data reveals where the actual complexity inflection points are for DFW market specifically. Thresholds are market-configurable: DFW defaults may differ from NYC or rural markets as EvntCue expands.
Gate bypass for Plnr referrals
If a Plnr refers an Orgnz to EvntCue with a referral link, the gate is pre-answered — that Plnr is pre-populated as the match. Orgnz still confirms the assignment, but the recommendation step is skipped. Referral attribution tracked in events.referred_by_plnr_id.
Platform learning
Over time, events.plnr_declined records build a dataset of events that proceeded without a Plnr. Cross-referencing post-event NPS scores and vendor issue rates against declined-gate events gives Cue the data to refine threshold recommendations. Events with high NPS and no issues suggest the threshold was too aggressive. Events with low NPS and issues suggest the Orgnz needed the Plnr.

DB schema additions

New fields on events table:
plnr_recommended boolean default false — true when gate fired and recommendation was shown
plnr_declined boolean default false — true when Orgnz chose to proceed without Plnr
plnr_gate_tier text — which tier the gate fired at ('diy', 'day_of', 'partial', 'full_service', 'corporate', 'destination')
plnr_gate_dismissed_at timestamptz — when Orgnz dismissed the gate (null if accepted or not yet shown)
referred_by_plnr_id uuid references tenants(id) — Plnr who referred this Orgnz, bypasses gate
is_destination_event boolean default false — set when event address > admin-configurable radius from Orgnz home

Cross-references: §9 (Cue system prompt switching) · §25 (Orgnz with Plnr assigned — companion mode) · §26 (event intelligence — vendor categories unlock after gate resolves) · §40 (planning layer sub-roles and budget thresholds)
43 — Plnr CRM & client lifecycle
From first inquiry to lifetime client — the relationship layer inside Plnr Pro
Most event Plnrs work without a CRM. They manage inquiries in email, track clients in spreadsheets, and rely on memory for follow-up. EvntCue’s Plnr CRM turns every completed event into a structured client record with significant dates, upcoming life milestones, and a re-engagement engine that surfaces future event opportunities automatically. The goal is not just to book one event — it is to own the lifetime of every client relationship a Plnr builds on the platform.
Privacy note: CRM records for clients who have not created an EvntCue account require explicit consent before data is stored. When a Plnr adds a new client record, the platform automatically sends a consent email to that client. Until consent is received, only name and event notes are stored — no personal data. See §45 for full consent flow and CCPA/TDPSA compliance architecture.

Client record — what the Plnr CRM stores

FieldTypePurposeConsent required?
client_nametextDisplay name for the CRM recordNo — business contact data
client_emailtextContact for consent email + future outreachConsent email triggers on save
client_phonetextOptional secondary contactYes — stored after consent
significant_dates[]jsonb array: {label, date, remind_days_before}Anniversary, birthday, children’s birthdays, vow renewal date, business founding date — any date the Plnr wants Cue to surface as a future event opportunityYes — stored after consent
upcoming_events[]jsonb array: {event_type, estimated_date, notes, status}Known future events the client has mentioned — “they mentioned a baby shower next spring” — Plnr manually adds, Cue surfaces as the date approachesNo — Plnr’s notes about their business
event_history[]linked event IDsAll events this client has had on the platform. Auto-populated from completed events. Gives Plnr full context before each new engagement.No — platform data
style_tags[]text arrayStyle preferences carried from past mood boards — “garden romantic, candlelit, no balloons”No — Plnr’s professional notes
budget_tierenum: budget / mid / luxuryPlnr’s assessment of typical spend level. Informs future proposal starting points.No — Plnr’s professional notes
referral_sourcetextHow did this client find the Plnr? Instagram, past client, venue referral, EvntCue marketplace. Used for Plnr marketing insights.No
plnr_notestextFree-form field. Dietary restrictions, family dynamics, decision-making style, what went wrong last time, what made them cry happy tears.No — Plnr’s professional notes

Significant dates — the re-engagement engine

This is where the CRM becomes an acquisition engine. Cue monitors significant dates across all Plnr client records and surfaces a re-engagement prompt to the Plnr when a date is approaching. The Plnr doesn’t have to remember — the platform remembers for them.
Anniversary (1 year)
90 days before the first anniversary, Cue prompts the Plnr: “[Client names]’ first anniversary is in 90 days. A lot of couples do a private dinner or vow renewal. Want me to draft a check-in message?” Plnr taps yes, Cue generates a warm, personalized outreach email using style tags and event history from the client record.
Upcoming events noted
When a Plnr logged “mentioned baby shower next spring” in upcoming_events, Cue resurfaces it in January with: “[Client name] mentioned a baby shower around spring. Want to reach out?” The Plnr doesn’t have to remember the conversation from nine months ago. The platform holds the thread.
Milestone birthdays
Significant dates can include children’s birthdays with a “milestone” flag for round numbers (10, 13, 16, 18, 21). Cue surfaces these 120 days out: “[Client name]’s daughter turns 16 in April. Milestone birthday parties in DFW often book 3–4 months out. Want to reach out now while dates are open?”

Outbound mood board & proposal builder

The current platform spec has mood boards as inbound — Orgnzs pin images, Plnrs and vendors see them. This section adds an outbound flow: the Plnr builds a curated proposal package including a mood board and sends it to a prospective client before that client has created an EvntCue event. This is a lead conversion tool, not a planning tool.
Building the outbound package
Plnr selects a CRM client record (or creates a prospect record). Selects event type and approximate budget range. Cue pre-populates a mood board with style-matched images based on the client’s style tags and past event history. Plnr edits the board — adds, removes, reorders. Plnr selects a service package from their configured offerings (Full-service, Partial, Day-of). Package price is auto-populated from the Plnr’s rate card. Plnr adds a personal note and hits “Send proposal.”
What the prospect receives
A branded email from the Plnr (Plnr’s name + EvntCue infrastructure, not EvntCue-branded) with a link to a proposal microsite: mood board images, service package description, pricing, Plnr bio and portfolio link, and a single CTA: “Accept this proposal” or “Schedule a call.” Accepting creates an EvntCue event automatically, assigns the Plnr, and fires the consent flow for the CRM record. The Orgnz is now on the platform.
Mood board image sourcing
Images are sourced from: (1) Plnr’s own past event portfolio (uploaded to their Plnr profile), (2) Unsplash / Pexels via API (free commercial license — no copyright exposure), (3) Orgnz’s past mood board pins if a prior event exists. Images from Pinterest or other web sources are explicitly not supported in the outbound builder — copyright liability is on the platform when it facilitates distribution.
Proposal tracking
Plnr sees when the prospect opened the proposal email, how long they spent on the microsite, whether they viewed pricing, and whether they forwarded it to someone else (partner, parent). Cue surfaces engagement signals: “[Prospect] opened your proposal 3 times in the last 48 hours but hasn’t responded. Want me to draft a follow-up?” This is the CRM-lite intelligence the rest of the Vndr ecosystem also gets in V2.

DB schema — new tables

plnr_clients (id, plnr_tenant_id, client_name, client_email, client_phone, significant_dates jsonb, upcoming_events jsonb, style_tags text[], budget_tier, referral_source, plnr_notes, consent_status enum(pending|granted|declined), consent_granted_at, created_at)

plnr_proposals (id, plnr_tenant_id, client_id references plnr_clients, event_type, budget_range_cents, mood_board_images jsonb, package_id, personal_note, sent_at, opened_at, microsite_time_seconds, accepted_at, resulting_event_id references events)

RLS: plnr_clients rows visible only to the Plnr who created them. No cross-Plnr access. plnr_proposals same policy. Client consent status is read-only to the Plnr — only the platform can write it (via consent email response).
Attorney review required: Consent flow language, opt-out mechanics, and data retention policy for plnr_clients records must be reviewed by a licensed attorney before launch. Texas TDPSA (effective July 2024) and California CCPA both apply to client records stored on non-platform individuals. See §45 for full legal framework.
44 — Co-Plnr collaboration
Lead Plnr & Co-Plnr on a single event — destination partners, capacity sharing, and network referrals
A single event can now have two Plnrs: a Lead Plnr who owns the client relationship and a Co-Plnr who handles a defined scope — typically the destination leg, the day-of execution, or an overflow event when the Lead is at capacity. The commission split is negotiated between Plnrs on-platform, logged in commission_flows, and processed through Stripe Connect. EvntCue facilitates the split but is not a party to the arrangement. This architecture solves three problems simultaneously: destination events, at-capacity referrals, and Plnr network building.

Three reasons a Co-Plnr is assigned

Destination event — local partner
The Lead Plnr is in DFW. The event is in Cabo, Tuscany, or Hawaii. The Lead needs a local partner with venue relationships, vendor knowledge, and boots-on-the-ground credibility. The Co-Plnr is a verified EvntCue Plnr in or near the destination with the destination_specialist tag and geographic coverage matching the event location. Lead retains the client. Co-Plnr handles local execution. Commission split is negotiated between them.
At-capacity referral
The Lead Plnr has toggled “At capacity” (§39, automated at-capacity responder). A new inquiry comes in. Instead of rejecting it, the Lead refers it to a trusted Co-Plnr from their network. The referring Lead gets a negotiated referral fee. The Co-Plnr takes the event. The Orgnz gets a warm handoff to a trusted professional, not a cold rejection. All of this stays on the EvntCue platform — 2.5% platform fee is retained on the Co-Plnr’s bookings.
Scope split on complex events
A large multi-day corporate event assigns one Plnr as Lead (logistics, vendor coordination) and a Co-Plnr as the on-site production manager (AV, staging, show calling). Both see the same event file. Commission is split by scope — Lead takes the planning fee, Co-Plnr takes the production fee. The Orgnz sees one unified dashboard and one primary contact (the Lead).

What each Plnr sees and can do

CapabilityLead PlnrCo-Plnr
Full event file (budget, guest list, timeline)Full accessFull read access — write access to their assigned vendor categories only
Vendor bookingsAll categoriesAssigned categories only — defined at Co-Plnr assignment
Mood boardFull editView + suggest (suggestions require Lead approval)
Orgnz communicationPrimary contact — all Orgnz-facing messagesNo direct Orgnz contact unless Lead grants it — Orgnz sees one Plnr
Commission visibilitySees full commission ledger including Co-Plnr splitSees only their own commission flows
CRM client recordFull access — Lead owns the client relationshipNo access — Co-Plnr cannot see or edit Lead’s CRM records
Cue accessFull Cue access for the eventCue access scoped to their assigned categories — cannot see Lead’s full event context

Commission split mechanics

The Lead Plnr proposes a split percentage when they invite the Co-Plnr. The Co-Plnr can accept or counter-propose. Once agreed, the split is locked to the event and stored in plnr_collab_agreements. When the Orgnz pays a service fee to the Plnr account, Stripe Connect automatically distributes the agreed split — no manual transfer, no float, no EvntCue holding funds.
Domestic split example
Lead Plnr charges the Orgnz a $4,500 planning fee. Co-Plnr (at-capacity referral taking the full event) receives 85% — $3,825. Lead retains 15% referral fee — $675. Stripe Connect splits on payment. Both Plnrs receive 1099-K from Stripe at year-end if threshold met. EvntCue collects 2.5% platform fee on the full $4,500 before split.
Destination split example
Lead Plnr (DFW) charges $8,000 total. Co-Plnr (Cabo local partner) receives 40% for local execution — $3,200. Lead retains 60% for pre-production, vendor selection, Orgnz relationship — $4,800. EvntCue collects 2.5% platform fee on $8,000. Stripe Connect handles the split. The Co-Plnr’s Stripe account must be onboarded to EvntCue before any split can be processed.
Legal note — money transmission: EvntCue never holds funds during the split. Stripe Connect processes the transfer directly between two Stripe accounts. This is the architecture that keeps EvntCue out of money transmitter licensing requirements in all 50 states. Any change to this architecture — EvntCue holding funds and distributing manually — would require state-by-state money transmitter licenses. Do not deviate from the Stripe Connect split model. Attorney review recommended before launch to confirm ToS language correctly frames EvntCue as infrastructure, not an intermediary to the referral arrangement.

Destination Cue intelligence — what Cue can and cannot do

When an event is flagged as a destination event, Cue activates a destination intelligence mode. This extends Cue’s capabilities with research and logistics tools while being explicit about what requires a human Co-Plnr.
TaskCue can handleRequires Co-Plnr
Permit research for destinationResearch local permit requirements, generate a permit checklist for the destination jurisdiction, flag Texas TABC doesn’t apply — destination alcohol licensing doesFiling and local compliance follow-through
Vendor sourcingSearch for destination-tagged Vndrs on platform, surface travel_willing vendors from DFW willing to travel, pull external affiliate links for destination vendor categories with thin local coverageLocal ground-truth vetting — Cue doesn’t know which Cabo caterer’s reviews are real
Guest travel logisticsGenerate hotel room block checklist, flight window recommendations based on event dates, guest travel information sheet template, airport transfer logistics outlineNegotiating hotel group rates, securing room blocks
Multi-timezone run-of-showGenerate run-of-show with timezone adjustments — DFW Lead sees CST, destination Co-Plnr sees local time, vendors see vendor-local timeDay-of execution in the destination timezone
Budget benchmarkingFlag that DFW market rates in budget_benchmarks do not apply — prompt Plnr to adjust expectations and confirm destination market rates with local Co-PlnrDestination market rate knowledge
International vendor contractsFlag that contracts with international vendors fall outside EvntCue payment protection and U.S. jurisdiction — recommend direct contracts between Plnr and vendorLocal contract law compliance
Currency and taxFlag currency conversion requirements, surface that international payments are outside Stripe Connect coverage, recommend wire transfer or local payment for international vendorsActual currency transactions

Plnr network — how Co-Plnrs get discovered

Cue-matched Co-Plnrs
When a Lead Plnr needs a Co-Plnr, they can ask Cue to find a match. Cue filters the Plnr database by: event type specialization, geographic coverage (destination or local), sub-role (day-of, production manager, etc.), availability on event date, and platform performance score. Up to 5 candidates returned. Lead invites their preferred match directly from the results.
Trusted network list
Each Plnr can maintain a private trusted network — a shortlist of Co-Plnrs they’ve worked with and trust. When they need a Co-Plnr for a new event, Cue checks the trusted network first before running a broader platform search. A Plnr’s trusted network is private — not visible to other Plnrs or to Orgnzs. This is the professional relationship layer that keeps Plnrs on the platform long-term.

DB schema — new tables

plnr_collab_agreements (id, event_id, lead_plnr_id, co_plnr_id, scope_categories text[], lead_pct numeric(5,2), co_plnr_pct numeric(5,2), agreed_at, status enum(pending|active|completed|disputed))

plnr_trusted_network (id, plnr_id, trusted_plnr_id, added_at, notes) — private per Plnr, no cross-visibility

Events table additions: co_plnr_tenant_id uuid references tenants(id) · collab_agreement_id uuid references plnr_collab_agreements(id)

RLS: plnr_collab_agreements visible to both Lead and Co-Plnr on the event. plnr_trusted_network visible only to the Plnr who owns the list.
45 — Privacy & data governance
50-state legal framework — what must be in place before launch
EvntCue operates in all 50 U.S. states from day one. The features introduced in v18.0 — Plnr CRM records for non-platform clients, Co-Plnr commission splits, outbound proposal emails, and destination vendor contracts — create specific legal obligations that must be addressed before any of these features ship. This section is the build record and attorney briefing document for those obligations. It is not a substitute for legal advice.
Attorney review required before launch — three items flagged explicitly: (1) CRM consent flow and data retention language for plnr_clients records. (2) ToS framing of Co-Plnr commission splits as a Plnr-to-Plnr arrangement with EvntCue as infrastructure only. (3) International booking disclaimer scoping out EvntCue’s payment protection for contracts with vendors outside U.S. jurisdiction. These are the three areas where the cost of attorney review is measured in hundreds of dollars and the cost of not doing it is measured in regulatory exposure across multiple states.

Applicable privacy laws by state

LawStateEffectiveEvntCue exposureRequired action
TDPSATexasJuly 2024High — primary launch market. All DFW Orgnzs, Plnrs, and Vndrs are Texas residents. CRM records for Texas residents are in scope.Privacy notice on platform. Opt-out mechanism. Data Processing Agreement (DPA) between EvntCue and Plnrs as controllers of CRM data. Data deletion on request within 45 days.
CCPA / CPRACalifornia2020 / 2023Medium — any California resident using the platform or stored in a Plnr’s CRM is in scope. California applies even if EvntCue is a Texas company.Same as TDPSA plus: “Do Not Sell or Share My Personal Information” link in footer. Opt-out honored within 15 business days. No selling personal data to third parties (EvntCue does not — confirm in privacy policy).
VCDPAVirginiaJan 2023Low at launch — DFW focus means few Virginia residents initially. Grows as platform expands.Same framework as TDPSA. DPA with Plnr controllers. Opt-out and deletion rights.
CPAColoradoJul 2023Low at launch. Same trajectory as Virginia.Same framework.
All other states38 statesVarious / pendingLow — no comprehensive privacy law currently. FTCA Section 5 (unfair or deceptive practices) still applies.Privacy policy must be accurate and not misleading. No deceptive data practices.

Data architecture — where data lives and who controls it

EvntCue as processor
For plnr_clients CRM records, the Plnr is the data controller — they decided to collect the client’s data and they determine what it’s used for. EvntCue is the data processor — the platform stores and processes the data on the Plnr’s behalf. This requires a Data Processing Agreement (DPA) in the Plnr’s terms of service. The DPA specifies that EvntCue processes CRM data only as instructed by the Plnr, never for EvntCue’s own purposes.
EvntCue as controller
For all platform event data — Orgnz profiles, event records, booking history, Cue interactions — EvntCue is the data controller. EvntCue determines why and how data is collected. The privacy policy governs this data. Orgnzs, Plnrs, and Vndrs who create accounts consent to the privacy policy at sign-up. Deletion requests must be honored within the applicable state law window.
Data residency
Supabase on AWS us-east-1 (North Virginia) is the default data residency. This is compliant for all 50 U.S. states. If EvntCue expands to Canada or EU, data residency must be re-evaluated — GDPR requires EU data to remain in the EU or in an adequacy-designated country. Do not migrate to EU-hosted infrastructure without legal review.

CRM consent flow — step by step

StepActionPlatform behaviorData stored
1Plnr adds a new client to CRMRecord created with consent_status: pending. Only name and Plnr notes stored. Personal data fields (phone, significant dates, birthdays) are locked.Name, plnr_notes only
2Platform sends consent email to clientAutomated email from Plnr’s name (not EvntCue brand): “[Plnr name] uses EvntCue to manage client relationships. They’ve added you to their client list. Your information is stored per our privacy policy. Click to opt out if you prefer not to be included.”Email logged, timestamp stored
3aClient takes no action (14 days)After 14 days without opt-out, consent_status updates to granted by inaction. This is the opt-out model (not opt-in) permitted under TDPSA and CCPA for B2B professional relationship data. All fields unlock for Plnr.Full record unlocks
3bClient clicks opt-outconsent_status updates to declined. All personal data fields are deleted within 24 hours. Only name and event history (platform data) is retained. Plnr notified.Name + event IDs only
4Client requests deletionFull record deleted within 45 days (TDPSA) or 45 days (CCPA). Event history records that are EvntCue platform data are retained for financial record-keeping per §36 data retention policy. Only the CRM-specific personal data is deleted.Event records retained; CRM data deleted

Commission split — ToS language required

The Co-Plnr commission split architecture (§44) requires specific ToS language to protect EvntCue from being classified as a party to a referral fee arrangement or as a money transmitter. The following framing must be reviewed and confirmed by a licensed attorney before launch:
Required ToS framing (draft — attorney review required): “Commission split arrangements between Lead Plnrs and Co-Plnrs are agreements between those parties only. EvntCue provides payment infrastructure (via Stripe Connect) to facilitate the agreed split but is not a party to the referral arrangement, does not determine split percentages, and does not hold funds at any point during the transaction. Plnrs are responsible for complying with any applicable state laws governing referral fee arrangements in their jurisdiction.”

International booking disclaimer — destination events

Required ToS language (draft — attorney review required): “For events held outside the United States, contracts between Plnrs and vendors in the destination jurisdiction are direct agreements between those parties and are governed by the laws of the destination jurisdiction. EvntCue’s payment protection, dispute resolution, and commission intelligence tools apply only to transactions processed through the EvntCue platform within U.S. jurisdiction. EvntCue does not facilitate payment to international vendors and is not responsible for disputes arising from contracts governed by foreign law.”

Mood board copyright — outbound proposals

The outbound proposal builder (§43) uses images from three sources. The platform’s copyright exposure differs by source:
