46 — Dual language & cultural intelligence
Spanish/English — the DFW market position no competitor has claimed
DFW has one of the largest Spanish-speaking populations in the United States. Quinceañeras, multigenerational Mexican-American weddings, corporate events for bilingual companies, and cultural celebrations are being planned by Orgnzs and executed by Vndrs who are currently served by platforms built entirely in English, by people who don’t understand the cultural context of what they’re planning. Dual language on EvntCue is not a translation feature — it is a market position. No competitor has claimed it. It is available today through the Claude API. It costs almost nothing to implement. And it will generate word-of-mouth that no marketing budget can buy.
The thesis: A Spanish-speaking Orgnz planning a quinceañera who gets Cue responding in fluent, culturally-aware Spanish — not translated English, but actual Spanish that knows what a vals de quince is and where it falls in the run-of-show — will tell every other parent in their network. Every food truck operator, every mariachi booking, every quinceañera Catr who gets onboarded in their own language is a supply-side anchor that no DFW competitor can easily replicate.

Implementation architecture — language is a rendering layer, not a data change

Language preference on tenant profile
tenants.language_preference enum('en', 'es') — set at onboarding, changeable in settings. Default: browser locale detection with manual override. All other data (event records, vendor names, dates, amounts) is stored in a single schema. Language is a rendering decision, not a storage decision. No duplicate tables, no parallel schemas.
Frontend i18n
Next.js with next-intl or react-i18next. All UI strings are i18n keys. English is the source language — Spanish translations are a JSON file per portal. Static UI strings (button labels, navigation, status messages, error copy) are translated at the framework level. Dynamic content (vendor names, event notes, proposal text) is stored as-entered.
Cue in Spanish
When tenants.language_preference = 'es', Cue’s system prompt includes language: es and a cultural context injection. Claude handles the rest natively — no separate Spanish model, no translation API. Cue responds in Spanish across all nine touchpoints. The system prompt also injects cultural intelligence flags for the event type (see below).

Cultural intelligence — events Cue understands that no competitor does

Event typeCultural context Cue knowsPlatform impact
QuinceañeraVals de honor (first waltz with chambelanes), surprise dance (choreographed by the quince), brindis timing, court coordination (14 chambelanes + 14 chambelanas), the changing of shoes ceremony, last doll, tiara and last toy traditions, Mass timing if religious ceremony. Run-of-show order is culturally prescribed — Cue knows the sequence.Event type template generates a culturally accurate run-of-show automatically. Chambelanes coordinator is surfaced as a vendor category. Cue prompts for Mass venue separately from reception venue.
Mexican-American weddingEl lazo (unity cord ceremony), arras (13 coins), traditional Mariachi during cocktail hour vs. reception, multi-day celebration structure (ensalada de novios, boda civil, boda religiosa, recepción), padrino/madrina system for vendor sponsorship, Hora Loca timing.Padrino/madrina system is a real budget offset mechanism — Cue can track sponsor contributions against vendor line items. Mariachi is surfaced as a separate vendor category from DJ. Multi-day events generate multi-day timelines automatically.
Padrino/madrina sponsorship systemIn traditional Mexican celebrations, specific vendors (cake, flowers, DJ, photography, alcohol) are sponsored by padrinos and madrinas — family members or close friends who cover individual line items as their gift. This is not a tip or a donation — it is a formal commitment made months in advance with specific vendor scope attached.New schema: event_sponsors (id, event_id, sponsor_name, sponsor_type enum(padrino|madrina), vendor_category_covered, amount_committed_cents, amount_received_cents, status). Cue surfaces padrino commitments in budget tracking — a sponsored line item is marked as covered and excluded from the Orgnz’s out-of-pocket budget. Budget benchmarking flags if a padrino commitment exceeds the typical vendor rate for that category.
Corporate bilingual eventsBilingual emcee requirement, simultaneous translation needs, Spanish-language signage and programs, cultural sensitivity for mixed-audience entertainment. DFW has dozens of companies with bilingual workforces holding annual events, holiday parties, and award ceremonies.Corporate event template gains a “Bilingual event” flag. Cue surfaces bilingual emcee as a vendor sub-role. Signage & Print category gains a Spanish-language collateral note.

Supply-side impact — Spanish-first Vndrs and Catrs

Many of the best DFW food truck operators, cultural performers, specialty caterers, and mariachi groups are Spanish-first businesses. They are not on The Knot or WeddingWire because those platforms were not built for them. EvntCue onboards them in their language, with a profile that shows their work accurately, and surfaces them to the Orgnzs who are specifically looking for what they do. This is a supply-side moat that compounds — every Spanish-first vendor who joins brings their network of referrals with them.

New schema additions

tenants.language_preference enum('en', 'es') default 'en'

event_sponsors (id, event_id, sponsor_name, relationship_to_orgnz, sponsor_type enum(padrino|madrina|family|corporate), vendor_category_covered, description, amount_committed_cents, amount_received_cents, status enum(committed|partial|received|cancelled), notes, created_at)

Event type library additions: Quinceañera template updated with cultural run-of-show sequence, chambelanes coordinator vendor category, padrino/madrina budget offset fields. Mexican-American wedding template added as distinct from standard wedding — multi-day structure, lazo/arras ceremony notes, Hora Loca timing flag.
47 — Mood board elevation
From image pinning to visual intelligence — six upgrades that make people tell their friends
The current spec has mood boards as inbound image pinning — Orgnz pins images, Plnr sees them, vendor suggestion engine reads style tags. That is the baseline. This section specifies six upgrades that transform the mood board from a reference tool into the emotional and operational center of the Orgnz experience. Each upgrade is independently buildable. Together they create something no competitor has.

Upgrade 1 — Cue reads the board and talks back

After an Orgnz pins five or more images, Cue analyzes the visual content using Claude’s vision API and surfaces a narrative interpretation — not a list of tags, a felt description of the event aesthetic.
What Cue does not say
“Your style tags are: garden, romantic, candlelit, organic.”
What Cue says instead
“The images you’ve pinned are telling a story — soft light, organic textures, peonies over roses, intimate scale. This feels like an evening garden party more than a formal reception. Does that resonate? Because it changes which florists I’d suggest and how I’d brief the lighting designer.”
The Orgnz responds. Their response refines Cue’s understanding and is stored as a natural-language style note alongside the image tags. This note becomes the vendor brief foundation. Cue fires this analysis automatically after pin count crosses 5, 10, and 20 images — each threshold prompts a deeper interpretation as the board develops.

Upgrade 2 — Auto-generated vendor brief per category

Every vendor inquiry currently sends a generic message. With the mood board as input, Cue generates a custom brief for each vendor category at the moment of inquiry. The vendor gets a brief that actually helps them quote accurately. The Plnr doesn’t write it. The Orgnz feels heard.
Vendor categoryExample auto-generated brief
Florist“The Orgnz’s mood board references organic garden arrangements with soft whites and blush, trailing greenery, no structured centerpieces. They responded positively to peonies and garden roses. Flagged: no balloon installations, no formal arrangements. Budget: $3,200 for florals. Event: April 12 reception for 80 guests at an outdoor venue.”
Lighting designer“Mood board is heavily candlelit and warm-toned. Orgnz specifically confirmed they want ‘evening garden party’ feel, not formal reception. No uplighting, no colored LEDs. String lights and warm wash. Budget: $1,800. Outdoor venue, 80 guests, April evening.”
Photographer“Style references: film-inspired, soft natural light, candid over posed. Orgnz’s exact words: ‘I want it to look like we didn’t know you were there.’ Portfolio match required for this inquiry — only photographers with documentary-style portfolios should receive this brief.”

Upgrade 3 — Living interactive board in the outbound proposal

When a Plnr sends a proposal package to a prospect (§43), the mood board in the microsite is not a static PDF. It is an interactive board. The prospect can swap images, flag ones they love, and add their own pins from a curated Unsplash/Pexels gallery scoped to their event type. When they accept the proposal and create their event account, their edited board becomes the starting point. The Plnr sees exactly what resonated and what didn’t before the first meeting. This replaces the “tell me what you like” first conversation with data.

Upgrade 4 — Style conflict detection

When an Orgnz’s board is pulling in contradictory directions, Cue notices and says so — before the Plnr has to navigate the conversation in a first meeting.
Conflict detected
“Your board has two strong directions pulling against each other — half of your pins are minimal and Scandinavian, half are maximalist and Moroccan. Both are beautiful, but they’re hard to do at the same time without losing both. Want me to show you which images are most consistent with each direction so you can decide which way to go?”
What this replaces
The first Plnr meeting where the client shows Pinterest boards in three completely different directions and the Plnr has to diplomatically ask them to choose. That conversation takes 45 minutes and sometimes ends with the client feeling judged. Cue has it first, in a low-stakes digital moment, and arrives at the first meeting with a resolved direction.

Upgrade 5 — Visual portfolio matching (image-level, not tag-level)

The current vendor suggestion engine matches mood board style tags to vendor profile tags. Upgrade 5 replaces tag-matching with visual matching using Claude’s vision API.
Tag-based matching (current)
Florist A tagged themselves “garden romantic.” Orgnz’s board is tagged “garden romantic.” Match. But Florist A’s portfolio is ballroom formal with loose greenery accents. The tag was aspirational, not accurate. The Orgnz books a call and is disappointed.
Visual matching (Upgrade 5)
Cue analyzes the visual content of Florist A’s portfolio photos and the Orgnz’s pinned images. The visual similarity score is low — different color temperature, different arrangement style, different scale. Florist B, who tagged themselves simply “organic” but whose portfolio photos visually match the Orgnz’s board, ranks higher. The Orgnz gets on the call and says “this is exactly what I was picturing.”
API note: Visual matching uses Claude’s vision capability via the Anthropic API — already in the stack. Vendor portfolio images are base64-encoded and compared against mood board images in a batch vision call. Cost estimate: ~$0.08 per vendor match pass. This runs at inquiry time, not continuously. Admin-configurable: visual matching can be weighted alongside (not replacing) the existing six-factor matching algorithm in §26.

Upgrade 6 — The “this is your event” moment

When all vendor categories are confirmed and the mood board is complete, Cue generates a single visual summary — a rendered illustration of the event’s aesthetic identity assembled from the confirmed vendor list and the mood board’s dominant visual language.
What it is
Not a photo. Not a mockup. A generated illustration in the style of the mood board — warm, soft, specific to this event. Includes: event name, date, venue name, and a one-line Cue description of the aesthetic (“an evening garden reception with candlelight and trailing peonies”). The Orgnz receives it as a shareable image when all vendors confirm.
Why it matters
The Orgnz shares it. Everyone who sees it asks what platform they used. Every share is an organic EvntCue impression. Every question is a potential new Orgnz. This is the viral acquisition moment — it uses the platform’s most emotional feature (the event is real, it’s coming together) at the platform’s most shareable moment (everything is confirmed). It works for weddings, quinceañeras, corporate galas, and milestones. Nobody has done this.
Implementation: Generated using Claude’s vision API to synthesize mood board aesthetic + a text-to-image prompt constructed by Cue from event details and style description. Alternatively, an SVG-based illustrated template system (faster to build, lower cost) where Cue selects the closest template from a curated set and personalizes it with event-specific details. The SVG approach is the V2 starting point — the fully generated illustration is a later enhancement. Both deliver the sharing moment. Start with SVG templates.

Implementation sequencing

UpgradePhaseAPI dependencyEstimated Cue cost addition
1 — Cue reads the boardMVPClaude vision API~$0.12 per analysis (fires at 5/10/20 pin thresholds)
2 — Auto vendor briefMVPClaude text API (existing)~$0.06 per inquiry brief — negligible
3 — Living interactive boardV2Unsplash/Pexels API + interactive canvasNo Cue cost — frontend feature
4 — Style conflict detectionMVPClaude vision API (same call as Upgrade 1)Included in Upgrade 1 cost — same API call
5 — Visual portfolio matchingV2Claude vision API (batch)~$0.08 per vendor match pass at inquiry time
6 — “This is your event” momentV2SVG template system (V2 start) / full generation (later)~$0.20 per event (fires once at full confirmation)
Upgrades 1, 2, and 4 are MVP because they use existing API capabilities and add the most emotional value at the lowest cost. They change what it feels like to use EvntCue from the first session. Upgrades 3, 5, and 6 are V2 because they require additional frontend infrastructure or higher API investment — but they are the features that create word-of-mouth and press coverage.

Three-vector pattern — locked v26.7

The same mood_boards + mood_board_items infrastructure carries three role-specific vectors:
RoleVectorLabel in UIQuestion
OrgnzWhat I wantMood Board“How do I want this to feel?”
VndrWhat I makeSignature Board“What kind of work do I do?”
VenuHow it feels hereAtmosphere Board“How does my space feel to host an event in?”

Atmosphere Board collapse pattern — locked v26.7

Once a Venu’s Atmosphere Board has 6+ photos uploaded AND the venue manager has affirmatively attested to ownership, the 9-cell upload grid auto-collapses to a horizontal thumbnail strip plus the four-dimension Cue read. An “Edit board” ghost-button in the card header re-expands. Architectural principle: form chrome should be busy where decisions are happening, quiet where decisions are settled. Apply this collapse pattern to other multi-input fields where ownership attestation gates completion (Vndr Signature Board on the same trigger; Orgnz Mood Board does not collapse because it’s perpetually being edited).

Custom-Cue color convention — locked v26.7

When a user adds custom content the platform did not suggest (custom inclusions, custom restrictions, manual category overrides), the resulting pill or chip renders in rose — the same color as the Cue voice block. The semiotic loop: the user’s specific contribution wears the same color as the platform’s interpretation engine, signaling that the platform is listening to your specific truth here. Distinct from suggested content (bay blue / coral / role accent).
48 — Marketplace liquidity strategy
How the supply side gets seeded before the first Orgnz creates an event
A marketplace only works if vendors, planners, and organizers all show up. That is harder than shipping software. The spec describes what the platform does when all five portals are populated — this section describes how they get populated. The cold-start solution for EvntCue is already built: the Vendor Bible, Venue Bible, Caterer Bible, and Entertainment Bible (to build) are Jason’s hand-curated DFW vendor lists. They are the supply-side seed. The liquidity strategy is the plan for turning those lists into live platform profiles before the first Orgnz event goes live.
The thesis from external market analysis: “We become the operating layer for private events where organizer, planner, venue, and vendors all need to coordinate in one shared system.” That thesis requires supply-side density in DFW before demand-side acquisition begins. You cannot demo the shared record with an empty marketplace. The liquidity strategy is not a marketing plan — it is a prerequisite for the product to work.

Phase 0 — Pre-launch supply seeding (before first Orgnz event)

ActionTargetSourceGoal
Venue onboarding blitz30 DFW venues across 5 capacity tiersExpanded Venue Bible (200+ venues)Every event type template has at least 6 venue options in DFW. Venue is the first booking — if venue results are thin, the entire event dies at step one.
Catr onboarding blitz20 DFW caterers across cuisine typesCaterer Bible (100+ caterers)At least 3 Catr options per cuisine type (American, BBQ, Mexican, International, Dietary-specific). Catr is the second-largest booking on most events.
Core Vndr onboarding15 photographers, 10 DJs, 8 florists, 6 AV companies, 5 transportationVendor Bible v2Minimum viable vendor density for a complete wedding. Plnr recommendation gate cannot function without Plnr options — but vendor categories cannot be filled without Vndr supply either.
Plnr founding cohort5–10 DFW Plnrs as founding membersJason’s professional network + direct DFW outreachFounding Plnrs get a permanent “Founding Plnr” badge on their profile. They help seed reviews, test the platform, and bring their existing client base. They are the platform’s most important early supply asset.
Spanish-first vendor outreach10 DFW mariachi groups, 5 quinceañera specialists, 8 cultural caterersJason’s catering background + DFW market knowledgeSeed the supply side for the Spanish-language market before any Spanish-language Orgnz creates an event. Critical for the dual-language market position to be credible, not cosmetic.

Minimum viable vendor density per event type

The platform is ready for an Orgnz to create an event when Cue can return at least 3 vendor matches per required category for that event type. Below 3 matches, Cue supplements with affiliate links (§26) and flags thin categories transparently. The following counts represent the minimum DFW supply needed before each event type goes live.
Event typeRequired categories at launchMin vendors per categoryReady when
WeddingVenue, Catr, Photographer, Florist, DJ/Band, Hair & Makeup, Transportation3 per category21 vendor profiles minimum across these 7 categories
QuinceañeraVenue, Catr, Photographer, DJ, Florist, Hair & Makeup, Chambelanes coordinator3 per category21 profiles including at least 3 chambelanes coordinators — the hardest to source
Corporate galaVenue, Catr, AV/Production, Entertainment, Photographer3 per category15 profiles minimum. Corporate can launch with a thinner vendor set than social events.
Birthday / milestoneVenue, Catr, Entertainment, Photographer3 per category12 profiles minimum. Simplest event type — first to go live after wedding.

The vendor onboarding value proposition — why they join before there’s demand

Free profile, real discovery
Every vendor tier has a free option. A vendor who joins before launch gets a live profile, a public portfolio, and availability calendar access at zero cost. They are visible to every Plnr and Orgnz who uses the platform. The cost of not joining is invisibility to a growing DFW event marketplace.
Founding vendor status
The first 100 vendors to complete their profile get a permanent “Founding Vendor” badge. This badge is visible on their profile forever and signals trust and early adoption. It is a status signal that cannot be purchased by later entrants. Its value increases as the platform grows.
Jason’s personal onboarding
Jason onboards the first vendors personally — a phone call or visit, not an email drip. His five years of DFW event operations means he knows these vendors, they know him, and the conversation is peer-to-peer, not sales. This is a competitive advantage that no funded startup can replicate. Use it fully before the platform scales past the point where personal onboarding is possible.

Demand-side sequencing — when Orgnzs get access

PhaseConditionAccess granted
Phase 0Supply seeding in progressFounding Plnrs only — closed beta. Plnrs can explore the platform, build their profiles, and configure their service offerings. No Orgnz access yet.
Phase 1Minimum viable vendor density met for at least 2 event typesFounding Plnrs invite their existing clients. Plnr-referred Orgnzs only — no public sign-up. This seeds the first real events with people who have a trusted relationship with their Plnr and high motivation to complete the booking flow.
Phase 25+ events completed end-to-end on platform, vendor density met for all primary event typesPublic Orgnz sign-up opens. Plnr recommendation gate goes live. Cue has real event data to learn from. Platform is ready for organic acquisition.
Phase 350+ events, 100+ vendor profiles, 10+ PlnrsVndr Pro and Catr Pro tiers promoted. Commission intelligence has meaningful data. Cue’s predictive insights start compounding. Platform-wide reviews visible publicly.

The liquidity flywheel once it starts

Once minimum viable density is reached, the marketplace is self-reinforcing. Every completed event generates reviews, which improve vendor trust scores, which improve vendor placement, which attracts more Orgnzs, which creates more events, which creates more reviews. Every Plnr who brings a client to the platform brings the client’s vendors with them — because the Plnr needs those vendors to join to use the booking flow. Every vendor who joins brings their Plnr relationships. The flywheel does not need to be pushed indefinitely — it needs to be started with enough initial mass that it can turn on its own. That initial mass is Jason’s vendor bibles and professional relationships. Use them.
Platform positioning sentence (from external market analysis, confirmed correct): “We become the operating layer for private events where organizer, planner, venue, and vendors all need to coordinate in one shared system.” This sentence belongs in the investor one-pager, the homepage, the founding Plnr outreach email, and every vendor onboarding conversation. It is the thesis. Everything else is evidence.
50 — Managed listings
Making external vendors look and feel internal — the supply-side onboarding engine
A managed listing is a vendor profile created and maintained by EvntCue on behalf of a real business that has given consent to be listed but has not yet created their own portal account. It is identical in appearance to a registered vendor profile — same card, same portfolio, same booking flow, same marketplace placement. The vendor has no login. EvntCue relays inquiries to them via email. Every managed listing is a warm acquisition target.
The strategic purpose: Managed listings eliminate the affiliate link gap. When a category is thin, instead of surfacing a “Shop online” badge that sends the Orgnz off-platform, Cue surfaces a real DFW business with a real profile. The Orgnz never leaves. The booking stays on platform. The vendor gets a client they didn’t have to find. EvntCue earns a booking commission instead of an affiliate cut. And when the vendor starts receiving real inquiries, the upgrade conversation writes itself.

Three-tier listing hierarchy

Registered
Vendor has their own portal login. Manages their own profile, packages, availability calendar, and commission slider. Full Vndr portal access. Receives booking requests directly in their dashboard. This is the target state for every vendor on the platform.
Managed
EvntCue manages the profile on the vendor’s behalf with their explicit consent. No portal login. Booking inquiries are forwarded via email relay. Vendor confirms via a simple response link. Profile is visually identical to registered — same card, same portfolio, same booking flow. “Managed by EvntCue” label visible to admin only, never to Orgnzs or Plnrs.
Affiliate
External link only. Surfaced as last resort when neither a registered nor a managed listing exists in the category and geographic area. Clearly badged “Shop online” in the UI — distinct from platform bookings. EvntCue earns affiliate commission, not booking commission. This is the state managed listings are designed to replace.

Consent requirement — non-negotiable

A business cannot be listed as a managed listing without explicit consent from the business owner or an authorized representative. Listing a business without their knowledge creates legal exposure and damages the relationship before it starts. The consent process is lightweight by design — a phone call followed by a confirmation email is sufficient.
Consent conversation script
“We’d like to feature your business on EvntCue and forward booking inquiries to you at no cost until you’re ready to create your own account. You’ll receive a platform fee only when a booking confirms — nothing upfront. Want us to set that up?” Nobody says no to that. It is free qualified leads with zero commitment.
Confirmation email
After verbal consent, EvntCue sends a confirmation email: “You’ve been added to the EvntCue marketplace as a managed listing. Booking inquiries will be forwarded to this email address. Reply to any inquiry to confirm or decline. To take control of your profile and access the full Vndr portal, create your account at [link].” Consent is logged with timestamp on the managed listing record.

Booking relay flow — how inquiries reach the vendor

StepActorWhat happens
1Orgnz / PlnrSelects managed listing from marketplace. Completes standard inquiry form — event date, guest count, budget, message. Identical to any registered vendor inquiry.
2EvntCueInquiry forwarded to vendor’s consent email address within 60 seconds. Email contains: event details, Orgnz name, event date, guest count, budget range, and a pre-built response link.
3VendorClicks “Accept and quote” or “Decline this inquiry” in the email. If accepting, enters their proposed price and any notes. No login required.
4EvntCueVendor response surfaced in the Orgnz / Plnr inbox as a quote. Orgnz/Plnr reviews and accepts. Deposit collected via Stripe. Booking confirmed.
5EvntCueVendor receives booking confirmation and payout details. Their managed listing profile is automatically updated with the booked date as blocked on the availability calendar.
6EvntCue (post-event)Review request sent to Orgnz. Review posted to managed listing profile. Vendor receives: “You now have [N] reviews on EvntCue. Create your account to respond, manage your profile, and access your full booking history.”

Upgrade conversion — the moment managed becomes registered

The conversion trigger: After a vendor’s second confirmed booking through their managed listing, EvntCue sends a personalized upgrade email: “You’ve received $[X] in bookings through your EvntCue managed profile. Take control — create your Vndr account to manage your availability, set your packages, configure your referral rate, and appear higher in search. It takes 10 minutes. [Create my account].” This is not a cold pitch. It is a warm offer to a vendor who has already made money through the platform.

Database schema additions

Table / fieldTypePurpose
tenants.listing_typeenum(registered, managed, affiliate)Controls booking flow, UI treatment, and relay logic. Default: registered for self-signed-up vendors.
managed_listings tableNew tableStores managed listing metadata: consent timestamp, consent email, relay email, consent confirmed boolean, upgrade prompted at, upgraded at.
managed_listing_relays tableNew tableLog of every inquiry forwarded and vendor response. Inquiry ID, sent at, responded at, response type (accepted/declined/no response), response notes.

Build priority

Phase 0 — seed managed listings
Before platform launch, Jason manually creates managed listings for DFW vendors from the vendor bibles who have given verbal consent. This seeds the marketplace with real vendor density before any registered vendor has signed up. Target: 50 managed listings across all categories before founding Plnr cohort gets access.
Phase 1 — relay infrastructure
Build the email relay system: inquiry → vendor email → response link → quote surfaced in Orgnz/Plnr inbox. This is the MVP booking flow for managed listings. Estimated 20–30 engineer hours.
Phase 2 — upgrade automation
Auto-trigger the upgrade prompt after second confirmed booking. Track conversion rate from managed to registered as a key platform health metric. The goal: every managed listing converts to registered within 6 months of their first booking.
51 — Vendor warm transfer
Turning booking declines into peer referrals — dead ends become warm leads
When a vendor declines a booking request today, the Orgnz gets a notification and starts over. That is a platform failure disguised as a normal outcome. The warm transfer converts every decline into a referral opportunity — the declining vendor nominates a peer, the peer gets a warm introduction, and the booking stays on platform. No competitor has this. It costs almost nothing to build and it changes the emotional experience of a decline from frustration to trust.
The mechanic: When a vendor clicks “Decline” on a booking request, a single optional prompt appears before the decline is submitted: “Know someone who might be a good fit for this event? Nominate them and we’ll send a warm introduction.” The vendor selects a peer from their trusted network or types a name. EvntCue routes the warm introduction to that vendor. The referring vendor earns a $25 platform fee credit when the referred booking confirms.

Flow — step by step

StepActorAction
1VendorReceives booking request. Reviews event details. Cannot fulfill — wrong date, at capacity, wrong scope, wrong budget.
2VendorClicks “Decline.” Optional warm transfer prompt appears. Vendor selects a peer from their EvntCue trusted network or types a business name. Adds an optional personal note: “Sarah does incredible work at this budget range.” Clicks “Send warm transfer and decline.”
3EvntCueForwards the original inquiry to the nominated vendor with a personal intro: “[Vendor name] thought you might be a great fit for this event and passed it along. Here are the details.” The nominated vendor receives this as a priority inquiry — visually distinct in their inbox.
4Orgnz / PlnrNotified that the original vendor declined but has recommended a peer. Peer’s profile surfaced immediately in the booking flow. Orgnz/Plnr sees it as a curated recommendation, not a dead end.
5Nominated vendorAccepts or declines. If accepted and booking confirms, original referring vendor receives $25 platform fee credit automatically applied to their next booking commission.

Why vendors use it

Reciprocity
Event vendor networks run on reciprocal referrals. A photographer who refers a florist expects the florist to return the favor. EvntCue makes this formal, trackable, and rewarded. Vendors who warm transfer frequently build stronger networks and receive more warm transfers in return.
Reputation
A vendor who just declines a booking leaves the Orgnz frustrated. A vendor who declines AND sends a warm transfer is remembered positively. Their profile gets a “Refers peers” badge visible on their public profile — a trust signal that says this vendor is embedded in the professional community.
Credit
$25 platform fee credit per confirmed warm transfer referral. A busy vendor who declines 10 bookings a month and warm transfers all of them earns $250 in credits — more than offsetting their $49 Pro subscription.

Database additions

Table / fieldPurpose
warm_transfers tableRecords every warm transfer: referring_vendor_id, nominated_vendor_id, original_booking_id, personal_note, status (pending/accepted/declined/confirmed), credit_issued boolean, created_at.
vendor_credits tablePlatform fee credit ledger. tenant_id, amount_cents, reason (warm_transfer_referral), booking_id, applied_at. Applied automatically at next booking commission calculation.
tenants.warm_transfer_countRunning total of confirmed warm transfers. Feeds the “Refers peers” badge threshold (minimum 3 confirmed transfers to display badge).
Build estimate: 15–20 engineer hours. Reuses existing inquiry, notification, and trusted network infrastructure. One of the highest ROI features in the platform relative to engineering effort. Ship in Phase 1b alongside the review system.
52 — Plnr velocity score
Not just how good — how fast. The metric that answers the question Orgnzs are actually asking.
Star ratings tell an Orgnz how good a Plnr is. The velocity score tells them how fast the Plnr moves — the metric that matters most for Orgnzs with a deadline. A Plnr who is 4.9 stars but takes 8 weeks to get all vendors confirmed is the wrong Plnr for someone planning a 10-week-out corporate event. The velocity score surfaces this information transparently so Orgnzs can match to the right Plnr for their timeline.

Three input signals

Time to venue confirmed
Measured from event creation to venue deposit collected. Averaged across all completed events. Fast Plnrs lock the venue in 3–7 days. Slow Plnrs take 3–4 weeks. This single metric predicts overall event pacing better than any other signal.
Time to fully booked
Measured from venue confirmation to all required vendor categories confirmed. Fast Plnrs complete this in 2–3 weeks. This measures how efficiently the Plnr works the marketplace — do they know who to call, or do they browse and compare for weeks?
Change request rate
Number of vendor changes after initial confirmation divided by total bookings. A proxy for expectation-setting quality. Plnrs who set client expectations poorly generate more changes. High change rate = slower timeline, higher Orgnz stress, more risk of cancellation cascade.

Score display

LabelTypical venue-to-booked timeWhat it means
Moves fastUnder 3 weeks totalPlnr has deep vendor relationships and makes decisions quickly. Best for tight timelines.
Steady pace3–6 weeks totalPlnr is thorough. Strong fit for events with 4+ months of lead time.
Deliberate6+ weeks totalPlnr is detail-oriented and does extensive research. Best for complex, high-budget events with long lead times.
Labels are neutral — none is framed as better than the others. A bride with 14 months of lead time may specifically want a Deliberate Plnr. A corporate coordinator with 6 weeks may need someone who Moves Fast. The score enables self-selection, not ranking.
Minimum data requirement: Velocity score only displays after a Plnr has completed 3 events on platform. Below that threshold the label reads “New to EvntCue — no velocity data yet.” This prevents a single slow event from permanently labeling a new Plnr, and prevents gaming by cherry-picking fast events.

Database additions

Field / viewPurpose
events.venue_confirmed_atTimestamptz. Set automatically when venue booking deposit clears. Already trackable from bookings table but needs denormalized onto events for fast aggregation.
events.all_vendors_confirmed_atTimestamptz. Set by trigger when all required vendor category bookings reach confirmed status.
plnr_velocity_summary viewAggregates per Plnr: avg days venue to confirmed, avg days confirmed to all booked, avg change request rate, completed event count, velocity label. Refreshed nightly via scheduled job.
Build estimate: 8–12 engineer hours. Primarily a data aggregation view and a UI label. No new booking logic required. Ship in Phase 2 once sufficient Plnr event data exists to make the score meaningful.
53 — Catr kitchen passport
Proof of execution at scale, not just certification on paper
A food handler certification tells you a Catr is licensed. It does not tell you whether they can execute a 400-cover plated dinner at a downtown Dallas hotel with a shared kitchen and a 45-minute setup window. The Kitchen Passport does. It is a Catr profile feature that automatically builds a verified record of every venue where the Catr has successfully executed an event — added at event completion, verified by the booking record, displayed on the Catr’s public profile.
The matching power: When a Plnr or Orgnz is booking catering for a specific venue, Cue filters the Catr marketplace by Kitchen Passport first — surfacing caterers who have already proven they can work that specific space. A Catr who has executed 8 events at The Adolphus ranks above an equally-rated Catr with no Adolphus history when the event is at The Adolphus. This is information no other platform tracks and no Catr can fake — it requires a completed, reviewed booking.

How it builds automatically

Event completes
When an event reaches “completed” status and the Catr booking has a confirmed review, the venue is added to the Catr’s Kitchen Passport automatically. No manual entry, no admin approval. The booking record is the proof.
Profile display
“Executed at 12 DFW venues” displayed on the Catr’s marketplace card. Clicking through shows the full list: venue name, event type, guest count range (not exact — privacy), and the date. Orgnzs and Plnrs can filter Catr search by venue name to find caterers with proven experience at their specific space.
Venue preferred list
Venues who see a Catr has executed multiple successful events at their space can add the Catr to their venue preferred vendor list (§34) with one tap. This creates a reciprocal visibility boost — the Catr appears in the venue’s suggested vendors and the venue appears in the Catr’s Kitchen Passport as a preferred relationship.

Database additions

TableKey fieldsPurpose
catr_kitchen_passportcatr_tenant_id, venue_tenant_id, event_id, event_type, guest_count_tier (under_50 / 50_150 / 150_300 / 300_plus), completed_at, review_idOne row per successfully completed and reviewed Catr event. Auto-inserted by trigger on event status → completed + review submitted. Never manually inserted.
catr_venue_preferredvenue_tenant_id, catr_tenant_id, added_at, added_by_user_idVenue-to-Catr preferred relationship. Feeds Cue’s Catr ranking for events at that venue.
Build estimate: 10–15 engineer hours. Trigger on event completion, aggregation query for passport display, filter on Catr search. Gets richer with every event that completes on the platform — a feature that earns its value over time rather than at launch. Ship in Phase 2.
54 — Mood board to vendor brief
Cue reads the board and writes category-specific briefs so vendors arrive aligned
When a Plnr shares a mood board with a vendor today, the vendor receives images. They interpret what they see and guess what the client wants. Sometimes they guess right. Often they spend the first consultation correcting misaligned expectations. The mood board to vendor brief eliminates that friction — the moment a Plnr shares the board with a specific vendor, Cue analyzes the images via the vision API and generates a category-specific brief written for that vendor type. The florist gets florist language. The photographer gets photographer language. The Plnr didn’t write it. Cue did.
This is §47 Upgrade 2 fully specced. The mood board elevation section already includes the auto vendor brief concept. This section provides the full implementation detail — the API call, the prompt architecture, the category-specific output format, and the UI treatment.

Example briefs by vendor category

Florist brief (generated)
“Based on this mood board, the client appears to prefer garden-style arrangements with cascading greenery, blush garden roses, and minimal white filler. 9 of 14 pinned images feature loose, organic arrangements. 0 images feature structured or formal designs. Suggest leading with an unstructured centerpiece option and avoiding tight, symmetrical arrangements. Color palette: blush, dusty rose, sage green, soft ivory. No red, orange, or deep purple tones appear in any pinned image.”
Photographer brief (generated)
“Mood board indicates strong preference for golden hour natural light and candid documentary-style coverage. 11 of 14 images are outdoor or window-lit. 0 images feature flash or studio lighting. Film-inspired editing tones appear in 8 pins. Client has pinned 0 posed formal portraits. Recommend discussing a first-look outdoor shoot and minimizing traditional family formal portrait time. Ceremony timing relative to golden hour is worth confirming with the couple.”

Technical implementation

Trigger
Fires when a Plnr clicks “Share board with [vendor name].” Cue makes a single vision API batch call with all board images plus a category-specific system prompt. Result cached on the mood_board_vendor_briefs table. Brief is regenerated only if new images are added to the board after initial share.
Prompt architecture
System prompt specifies the vendor category and instructs Cue to analyze color palette, arrangement style, lighting preference, formality level, and any category-specific signals. Output must be under 150 words, written in plain English, addressed directly to the vendor. Never uses design jargon the vendor might not recognize.
UI treatment
Brief appears as a blue callout card at the top of the shared board view the vendor sees when they open the link. Label: “Cue brief for [category] — based on this board.” Vendor can dismiss it. Plnr can edit it before sharing if Cue missed something. The brief is advisory, not authoritative.
TableKey fieldsPurpose
mood_board_vendor_briefsboard_id, vendor_tenant_id, vndr_category, brief_text, generated_at, edited_by_plnr boolean, plnr_edit_textCached brief per board per vendor category. Regenerated on board image change. Plnr edits stored separately so original Cue output is always preserved for comparison.
Build estimate: 15–20 engineer hours plus vision API cost (~$0.01–$0.03 per brief generation depending on image count). One of the highest emotional impact features in the platform. Ship in Phase 1b alongside the core mood board feature.
55 — Ghost event
A private planning sandbox that converts to a live event in one tap
The Ghost Event is an uncommitted, invisible event a user can create for free with zero intention to immediately book. They enter a date, guest count, rough budget, and event type. Cue populates a complete vendor suggestion list with estimated DFW pricing. The event is invisible to vendors — no inquiries sent, no availability checked. It is a planning sandbox. When the user is ready to make it real, one button press converts the Ghost Event into a live event, fires the venue gate, and collects the $19 pay-per-event charge. Everything they built in the sandbox carries over.
The strategic purpose: Ghost Events dramatically lower top-of-funnel friction. A user who is not ready to commit $19 today will create a Ghost Event because it is free and effortless. They invest time building their vision in the platform. By the time they are ready to book, they are 80% planned and emotionally invested. The $19 pay-per-event charge at conversion feels like unlocking something they already own, not a gate they have to pay to pass through.

What the Ghost Event includes

Full Cue vendor suggestions
Cue runs the full vendor matching engine and returns a complete suggested vendor list with estimated pricing for each category. The Orgnz can browse vendor profiles, save favorites, and build a shortlist — all without triggering a single inquiry. The shortlist persists and becomes the starting point when the event goes live.
Budget sandbox
Full budget calculator with category breakdown. Orgnz can adjust line items, see per-head cost, and experiment with trade-offs — “what if I cut the florist budget by $1,000 and upgrade the catering?” All budget work saves and carries over to the live event.
Mood board
Free tier mood board (10 pins) available in Ghost Event. Pins carry over to live event. This is the hook that creates emotional investment — a user who has spent 45 minutes building a mood board has skin in the game and will pay $19 to protect that work.
5 Cue messages
Same as the free tier. Enough for the user to get vendor suggestions and a budget breakdown. When they hit the limit inside a Ghost Event, the conversion prompt is even more compelling: “Go live to unlock 20 more Cue messages and start booking your shortlist.”

Conversion moment — Ghost to live

The “Make it real” button appears persistently at the top of the Ghost Event dashboard. When clicked: Stripe PaymentIntent for $19 fires. On payment success, event status changes from ghost to draft. Venue gate activates. Cue message count resets to 20. Saved vendor shortlist becomes the active suggestion queue. The Orgnz is now in the live booking flow with their work intact.
FieldGhost stateLive state (post-conversion)
event_statusghostdraft
Vendor inquiriesBlockedEnabled after venue confirmed
Vendor visibilityInvisible to all vendorsVisible on booking confirmation
Cue messages5 (free tier)20 (pay-per-event tier)
Mood board10 pins, no sharing25 pins, public link enabled
Budget workSaved, sandbox onlyCarries over, live budget tracker
Vendor shortlistSaved favorites onlyActive suggestion queue
Build estimate: 20–25 engineer hours. Requires adding ghost to the event_status enum, blocking inquiry and vendor visibility logic for ghost events, and the conversion payment flow. The Cue suggestion engine already runs — ghost events just need the booking layer suppressed. Ship in Phase 1c as the primary free-tier acquisition feature.
56 — EvntCue Live
The event doesn’t just get planned on EvntCue. It runs on EvntCue.
EvntCue Live is the day-of operational mode that activates automatically 24 hours before an event’s start time. Every confirmed vendor, the Plnr, and the Orgnz share one live dashboard. The timeline ticks in real time. Vendors check in on arrival. The Plnr pushes updates. When the ceremony runs 12 minutes long, every downstream vendor gets an automatic ripple notification adjusting their cue times. No phone calls. No group texts. No one misses the update.
This is the feature that makes EvntCue irreplaceable. A Plnr who coordinates a flawless event using EvntCue Live never goes back to WhatsApp group chats and printed timelines. A vendor who gets real-time ripple notifications instead of a frantic phone call at 4:47pm trusts the platform. An Orgnz who watches their event execute in real time on their phone while they enjoy cocktail hour becomes an evangelist. Build this and the retention problem solves itself.

Five core components

Vendor check-in
Every confirmed vendor receives a check-in prompt 30 minutes before their scheduled arrival time: “Tap to check in when you arrive on site.” The Plnr’s Live dashboard shows real-time check-in status: Florist — arrived 9:02am. Photographer — en route, ETA 9:20am. Catr — not yet checked in (flagged amber at 15 minutes past expected arrival, red at 30 minutes).
Live timeline
The run-of-show from §23 becomes a live ticking timeline. Current item highlighted. Next item shown with countdown. Plnr can advance, delay, or skip items with one tap. Every change ripples to affected vendors instantly via push notification. No vendor needs to watch the timeline — they only receive notifications when something changes that affects their slot.
Ripple notifications
The intelligent engine behind EvntCue Live. When the Plnr marks “Ceremony running 12 minutes late,” the system calculates every downstream impact: cocktail hour shifts, catering service time shifts, first dance shifts, cake cutting shifts. Each affected vendor gets a personalized notification: “Heads up — cocktail hour now starts at 5:12pm. Your setup window has shifted accordingly.”
Issue log
Plnr-only. Private log of everything that went wrong or needed intervention during the event. “Florist table 4 centerpiece wrong color — substituted 3:45pm.” “AV feedback issue resolved 6:12pm.” Visible only to the Plnr. Feeds into the post-event debrief report Cue generates. Over time this builds a personal playbook of what to watch for at each venue.
Live broadcast
Plnr can send a broadcast message to all vendors simultaneously: “We are running 8 minutes behind. Please hold your positions.” Or to a specific vendor: “Photography — family formals have been moved to the garden terrace.” In-app push + SMS fallback. Message log preserved on the event record.
Orgnz view
Simplified read-only view for the Orgnz. Shows current timeline position, next three items, and vendor check-in status. No operational controls — those belong to the Plnr. One button: “Message my Plnr.” The Orgnz sees that everything is under control without being in the way of the people running it.

Technical architecture

Real-time layer
Supabase Realtime subscriptions on the live_events and live_timeline_items tables. Every timeline update, check-in, and broadcast is a database write that broadcasts to all subscribers on that event instantly. No polling. No refresh required. PWA receives push notifications via Web Push API for background alerts when the app is not open.
Offline resilience
The day-of offline bundle from §23 includes a static snapshot of the run-of-show and all vendor contacts. If a vendor loses signal during the event, they have a local copy of their cue times and the Plnr’s mobile number. EvntCue Live requires connectivity to function fully but never leaves anyone stranded without basic information.

Database additions

TableKey fieldsPurpose
live_eventsevent_id, activated_at, current_timeline_item_id, is_running_late boolean, minutes_behind integer, status (standby/active/completed)Live event state. Activated automatically 24 hours before event start. Single row per event.
live_vendor_checkinsevent_id, vendor_tenant_id, expected_arrival, checked_in_at, check_in_method (app/sms), location_notesVendor arrival tracking. One row per vendor per event. Updated on check-in tap.
live_timeline_itemsevent_id, run_of_show_item_id, original_start_time, current_start_time, status (upcoming/active/completed/skipped), ripple_sent booleanLive state of each timeline item. Diverges from run-of-show when Plnr makes adjustments. Ripple notifications sent when current_start_time changes.
live_broadcastsevent_id, sender_id, message, recipient_type (all/vendor/plnr/orgnz), recipient_tenant_id, sent_at, delivery_method (push/sms)Full broadcast message log. Preserved on event record after completion.
live_issue_logevent_id, plnr_tenant_id, description, logged_at, resolved boolean, resolved_atPlnr-only private issue log. Feeds post-event Cue debrief.
Build priority — Phase 2, Month 7–9. EvntCue Live requires real event data to design correctly. The ripple notification logic needs to be calibrated against actual run-of-show deviation patterns from real events. Build it after 20+ events have completed on the platform and you have data on how timelines actually shift. Estimated 60–80 engineer hours. When it ships, it is the press moment — no other event platform has day-of real-time coordination for private events.
57 — Schema additions v25.0
All database tables confirmed in sessions after v24 — required before first build.
These tables were finalized across multiple design sessions and are not optional additions — they underpin features already specced in §39–56. Every table listed here must exist in the Supabase migration before any related UI is built. Schema costs nothing. Missing schema costs rewrites.

1 — landing_capture_sessions (§57 landing flow)

Anonymous pre-auth info capture from the landing page clock/timeline flow. When a visitor selects event type, guest count, date horizon, and budget before creating an account, those choices are stored here. On account creation the matching session is consumed and the new event is pre-filled. Conversion feels magical — no re-entry required.
ColumnTypeNotes
idUUID PKgen_random_uuid()
session_tokenTEXT UNIQUE NOT NULLRandom token stored in browser localStorage. Used to match session on account creation.
event_typeevent_type enumFrom the clock selector — Wedding, Corporate, Gala, etc.
guest_count_rangeTEXTChip selection: "Under 50", "50–100", "100–250", "250–500", "500+"
date_horizonTEXTTile selection: "<1 mo", "1–3 mo", "3–6 mo", "6–12 mo", "12+ mo"
budget_estimateINTEGERRaw dollar value from logarithmic slider. Default: 45000 (DFW avg $40,129 × 1.12).
portal_intentTEXT nullable"plnr" | "catr" | "vndr" | "venue" if user tapped a supply-side portal instead.
email_capturedTEXT nullableEmail entered at the gate before account creation, if any.
converted_user_idUUID nullable FK usersSet on account creation. Marks session as consumed.
expires_atTIMESTAMPTZcreated_at + 2 hours. Nightly pg_cron job deletes expired unconverted rows.
created_atTIMESTAMPTZDEFAULT NOW()
RLS: No RLS required — rows are anonymous. Service role only on server-side consumption at account creation. Index on session_token and expires_at.

2 — user_roles junction table (multi-role users)

Replaces the single role column on profiles. One person can hold multiple roles — a Plnr can also be a Vndr, a host can also be a vendor. The junction table makes this clean. The active role for a given session is stored in the session context, not on the user record.
ColumnTypeNotes
idUUID PK
user_idUUID FK users NOT NULL
tenant_idUUID FK tenants NOT NULLEach role has its own tenant record.
roletenant_type enum NOT NULLorgnz | plnr | vndr | catr | venue
is_primaryBOOLEAN DEFAULT FALSEThe role shown by default on login. User can switch.
created_atTIMESTAMPTZDEFAULT NOW()
Migration note: Existing single-role users get one row in user_roles on migration. The old role column on profiles is deprecated but kept read-only for 30 days as a fallback before drop.

3 — plnr_vendor_mutes (Plnr mute button §39)

The Plnr mute button allows suppression of a vendor at two scopes: event-level (this event only) or account-level (all future Cue suggestions). Muted vendors are excluded at the Cue prompt level — they are never scored or surfaced, not merely ranked lower. Orgnzs cannot see this table.
ColumnTypeNotes
idUUID PK
plnr_tenant_idUUID FK tenants NOT NULL
vndr_tenant_idUUID FK tenants NOT NULL
scopeENUM(event, account) NOT NULLevent = this event only. account = all future events for this Plnr.
event_idUUID FK events nullableRequired when scope = event. NULL when scope = account.
reasonTEXT nullableOptional free-text. Stored but never shown to vendor.
muted_atTIMESTAMPTZ DEFAULT NOW()
unmuted_atTIMESTAMPTZ nullableSet on unmute. Row kept for audit. Cue excludion lifted immediately.
RLS: Own Plnr tenant only. Vendor never reads this table under any circumstance. UNIQUE constraint on (plnr_tenant_id, vndr_tenant_id, scope, event_id) prevents duplicate mutes.

4 — tenant_certifications (Catr + Plnr cert uploads)

Catr is a hard-defined role: any food product being served. Required certifications before a Catr profile goes live: general liability insurance, food handler certification, and TABC certification for any event serving alcohol. Plnr profiles require a professional certification upload (optional but displayed as a trust badge). All certs have expiry dates — expired certs trigger profile suspension until renewed.
ColumnTypeNotes
idUUID PK
tenant_idUUID FK tenants NOT NULL
cert_typeENUM NOT NULLgeneral_liability_insurance | food_handler | tabc | plnr_professional | other
file_urlTEXT NOT NULLSupabase Storage URL. Admin-only bucket. Never public.
issued_dateDATE nullable
expiry_dateDATE nullableCron job checks daily. 30-day warning email. Profile suspended on expiry.
verifiedBOOLEAN DEFAULT FALSEAdmin manually marks verified after document review.
verified_byUUID FK users nullableAdmin user who verified.
verified_atTIMESTAMPTZ nullable
rejection_reasonTEXT nullableSet if admin rejects. Shown to tenant.
created_atTIMESTAMPTZ DEFAULT NOW()
Catr hard rule: A Catr tenant cannot receive booking inquiries until general_liability_insurance + food_handler certs are verified = TRUE. TABC required additionally if the event has alcohol = TRUE. This is enforced at the booking inquiry layer, not just the profile display layer. A Catr can onboard and browse but cannot be contacted until certs are cleared.

5 — vendor_trust_scores (no-ghosting enforcement §39)

Trust score is a computed value updated after every booking interaction. It feeds search placement — vendors with higher scores appear higher in Cue recommendations. Score below 60 triggers reduced placement. Below 40 triggers profile suspension pending review. The score is visible to vendors on their dashboard. It is not shown to Orgnzs or Plnrs as a raw number — they see a tier badge instead (Trusted, Verified, New).
ColumnTypeNotes
tenant_idUUID PK FK tenantsOne row per vendor/catr/venue tenant.
response_time_scoreNUMERIC(4,1)35% weight. Based on median hours-to-respond on inquiries.
completion_scoreNUMERIC(4,1)25% weight. Confirmed bookings / accepted inquiries ratio.
on_time_scoreNUMERIC(4,1)20% weight. EvntCue Live check-in data. Requires §56 to be built.
review_scoreNUMERIC(4,1)20% weight. Average star rating from confirmed post-event reviews.
composite_scoreNUMERIC(4,1)Computed: (response×0.35)+(completion×0.25)+(ontime×0.20)+(review×0.20). Updated after every booking event.
tierENUM(new, verified, trusted, suspended)new: <5 bookings. verified: score≥60. trusted: score≥80. suspended: score<40.
booking_countINTEGER DEFAULT 0Total confirmed bookings. Score meaningful after 5+.
last_computed_atTIMESTAMPTZUpdated on every booking status change.

6 — booking_status_log (full audit trail)

Every status change on every booking is logged with timestamp, actor, and reason. Required for financial integrity, dispute resolution, and commission reconciliation. Immutable — no deletes, no updates. Append-only.
ColumnTypeNotes
idUUID PK
booking_idUUID FK bookings NOT NULL

created_atTIMESTAMPTZ DEFAULT NOW()Immutable. No update/delete RLS policies on this table.

7 — cancellation_policies + event_cancellations

Each vendor and venue sets a cancellation policy at the profile level. The policy is attached to every booking at time of confirmation — a snapshot is stored so policy changes don’t retroactively affect existing bookings. Cancellations trigger a structured flow: reason logged, refund calculated per policy, Stripe refund initiated, trust score impact applied, both parties notified.
ColumnTypeNotes

trust_score_impactNUMERIC(4,1) nullableNegative delta applied to vendor trust score if vendor-initiated late cancellation.
created_atTIMESTAMPTZ DEFAULT NOW()
Rule: Orgnz-initiated cancellation >30 days out → full deposit refund minus platform fee. Orgnz-initiated <30 days → per vendor policy. Vendor-initiated cancellation at any time → full refund to Orgnz + trust score penalty. Force majeure flag available for admin override. All flows logged to booking_status_log.

10 — booking_inquiries (missing parent table — critical)

Referenced as a FK by managed_listing_relays and implied throughout the booking flow but never defined. This is the table that captures every vendor inquiry before it becomes a confirmed booking. Without it the migration fails on line one. Defined here as the canonical source.
ColumnTypeNotes
idUUID PKgen_random_uuid()
event_idUUID FK events NOT NULL
orgnz_tenant_idUUID FK tenants NOT NULLThe Orgnz or Plnr sending the inquiry.
vndr_tenant_idUUID FK tenants NOT NULLThe vendor, caterer, or venue receiving it.
event_dateDATE NOT NULLSnapshot of event date at time of inquiry.
guest_countINTEGER nullable
budget_range_low_centsINTEGER nullable
budget_range_high_centsINTEGER nullable
messageTEXT nullableFree-text from the Orgnz/Plnr to the vendor.
statusENUM NOT NULLpending | accepted | declined | expired | converted. expired = no response after 48hr. converted = booking confirmed from this inquiry.
responded_atTIMESTAMPTZ nullableSet when vendor accepts or declines.
expires_atTIMESTAMPTZ NOT NULLcreated_at + 48 hours. Cron sets status = expired if no response.
converted_booking_idUUID FK bookings nullableSet when inquiry converts to a confirmed booking.
created_atTIMESTAMPTZ DEFAULT NOW()
RLS: Orgnz/Plnr reads own event inquiries. Vndr reads inquiries where vndr_tenant_id = own tenant. Neither party sees the other’s inquiry history on other events. Admin reads all. Index on (event_id), (vndr_tenant_id), (expires_at) for the cron expiry job.

11 — events table — three missing fields

Three fields referenced in other sections that do not exist on the events table. All three are load-bearing for features already specced.
FieldTypeWhy it’s needed
has_alcoholBOOLEAN NOT NULL DEFAULT FALSERequired for TABC cert enforcement in tenant_certifications. A Catr serving alcohol at an event where has_alcohol = TRUE must have a verified TABC cert before they can receive an inquiry for that event. Without this flag the TABC gate cannot be implemented — it references a field that doesn’t exist.
venue_confirmedBOOLEAN NOT NULL DEFAULT FALSERequired for venue-first booking lock. All vendor inquiries are blocked (status forced to pending_venue_lock) until this flips TRUE. Set automatically when the venue booking transitions to confirmed status. Without this the venue-first lock exists only in prose — there’s nothing for the booking layer to check.
venue_booking_idUUID FK bookings nullableThe specific booking record for the venue. Needed to determine which booking to watch for the venue_confirmed flip. Without this the system cannot identify which booking represents "the venue" vs other vendors on the same event.
Migration: ALTER TABLE events ADD COLUMN has_alcohol BOOLEAN NOT NULL DEFAULT FALSE; ALTER TABLE events ADD COLUMN venue_confirmed BOOLEAN NOT NULL DEFAULT FALSE; ALTER TABLE events ADD COLUMN venue_booking_id UUID REFERENCES bookings(id) ON DELETE SET NULL; These are additive — no existing rows are affected.

12 — bookings table — escrow release fields (dual confirmation)

The dual-confirmation escrow release is described in four places across the document but has no structural implementation. Both parties must confirm before Stripe releases funds. These fields provide the state machine for that flow.
FieldTypeNotes
orgnz_confirmed_atTIMESTAMPTZ nullableSet when Orgnz/Plnr taps “Confirm service delivered.” Triggered post-event or post-delivery milestone.
vendor_confirmed_atTIMESTAMPTZ nullableSet when vendor taps “Confirm service completed.”
escrow_released_atTIMESTAMPTZ nullableSet by the server when both confirmations are present. Triggers Stripe capture. Never set manually.
escrow_release_methodENUM nullabledual_confirm | auto_release | admin_override | dispute_resolved. Immutable once set. Audit trail for every payout.
auto_release_atTIMESTAMPTZ nullableIf neither party confirms within 72 hours of event end date, escrow auto-releases to vendor. Protects vendor from Orgnz ghosting post-event.
amount_centsINTEGER NOT NULLTotal booking value in cents. Replaces any ambiguous amount float field. Canonical financial field on this table.
commission_amount_centsINTEGER NOT NULL DEFAULT 0Platform commission on this booking in cents. Calculated at confirmation. Stored immutably — rate changes don’t affect historical records.
vendor_payout_centsINTEGER NOT NULL DEFAULT 0amount_cents minus commission_amount_cents. What the vendor actually receives via Stripe Connect payout.
Escrow release rule: Server checks after every confirmation write — if both orgnz_confirmed_at IS NOT NULL AND vendor_confirmed_at IS NOT NULL AND escrow_released_at IS NULL → initiate Stripe capture, set escrow_released_at = NOW(), set escrow_release_method = dual_confirm. A pg_cron job checks daily for bookings past auto_release_at with escrow_released_at still NULL and auto-releases those. Admin override sets method = admin_override and logs to booking_status_log with reason required.

13 — commission_flows — amount_cents fix

The commission_flows table currently lists the financial field as amount with no type. This is corrected here. All financial values on this platform are stored as integers in cents. Float storage on commission values will cause penny-off errors that compound across thousands of transactions.
FieldCorrect typeReplace
amount_centsINTEGER NOT NULLReplaces untyped amount field. Rename in migration: ALTER TABLE commission_flows RENAME COLUMN amount TO amount_cents; ALTER TABLE commission_flows ALTER COLUMN amount_cents TYPE INTEGER USING (amount_cents * 100)::INTEGER;
basis_amount_centsINTEGER NOT NULLReplaces untyped basis_amount. The gross value the commission rate is applied to. Also in cents.

14 — RLS policies — three tables missing explicit rules

Three tables added in v25.0 have RLS callouts but no explicit policy logic. Defined here precisely enough for a developer to implement without guessing.
TableRead policyWrite policy
plnr_vendor_mutesOwn plnr_tenant_id only. No exceptions. Vendor cannot read this table under any policy.INSERT/UPDATE: own plnr_tenant_id only. DELETE: not permitted — use unmuted_at timestamp instead. Audit trail preserved.
tenant_certificationsOwn tenant_id reads own certs. Admin role reads all. No cross-tenant reads.INSERT: own tenant_id only (upload). UPDATE: admin only (verified flag, rejection_reason). DELETE: admin only. Tenants cannot delete their own cert records — they upload new versions instead.
booking_status_logOrgnz/Plnr reads logs for own events. Vendor reads logs for own bookings only. Admin reads all.INSERT: service role only (triggered by booking status change function). No UPDATE. No DELETE. Append-only enforced at the RLS layer, not just application layer.

15 — user_roles migration — explicit steps

The v25.0 user_roles table note said the old profiles.role column is deprecated but did not specify the migration path. Defined here explicitly.
Migration sequence:
1. CREATE TABLE user_roles (as defined in §57 item 2).
2. INSERT INTO user_roles (user_id, tenant_id, role, is_primary) SELECT id, tenant_id, role::tenant_type, TRUE FROM profiles WHERE role IS NOT NULL;
3. Add column: ALTER TABLE profiles ADD COLUMN role_deprecated TEXT; UPDATE profiles SET role_deprecated = role;
4. DROP COLUMN profiles.role — execute 30 days after step 3 once all application code reads from user_roles.
5. RLS on user_roles: user reads own rows. Admin reads all. No cross-user reads.

16 — event_memory_pages — orphaned pin FK fix

event_memory_pages.gallery_pin_ids is a UUID array with no FK constraint. If a mood board pin is deleted the memory page silently holds orphaned UUIDs that resolve to nothing. Fix: replace the array column with a junction table.

Summary — v25.0 complete schema additions: 9 new tables (landing_capture_sessions, user_roles, plnr_vendor_mutes, tenant_certifications, vendor_trust_scores, booking_status_log, cancellation_policies, event_cancellations, managed_listing_relays, event_memory_pages) + 1 junction table (memory_page_pins replacing orphaned UUID array) + 3 new fields on events (has_alcohol, venue_confirmed, venue_booking_id) + 8 new fields on bookings (escrow release state machine) + 2 financial field corrections on commission_flows (amount→amount_cents, basis_amount→basis_amount_cents) + explicit RLS policies for 3 sensitive tables + explicit user_roles migration sequence. 58 — Commission flow by user
Exactly who pays what, when, and where the money goes.
Every dollar on EvntCue follows a defined path. Two charge events per booking (deposit + remaining balance), two fee layers per charge (Stripe + EvntCue), one optional vendor commission dial, one cancellation fee structure. Nothing is ambiguous. This section is the source of truth for the payment engineer, the accountant, and the legal review.
Core rates (v25.1): EvntCue platform fee = 1% of each charge (deposit and remaining balance separately). Stripe processing = 2.9% + $0.30 per charge. Vendor commission = 0–20% dialed by vendor (optional, paid to EvntCue at payout). Cancellation fee = 5% of total booking value, retained by EvntCue. Stripe processing fees are always non-refundable — this is Stripe policy, not EvntCue policy, and must be disclosed to Orgnz at booking confirmation.

Phase 1 — Orgnz pays deposit

v26.4: The Orgnz pays a deposit to confirm the booking. Standard deposit is 25% of the total booking value, configurable per vendor. The charge hits immediately. EvntCue takes 2.5% of the booking value (the platform fee, earned at booking confirmation per §08). Stripe takes their 2.9% + $0.30 processing fee. The remainder sits in escrow until the event is finalized. Wallet model events (§55.5, Slice B): the platform fee is drawn from the event wallet at booking confirmation rather than from the deposit charge.
PartyActionAmount (example: $10,000 booking)
OrgnzPays deposit$2,500.00 (25% of $10,000)
StripeProcessing fee — non-refundable$72.80 (2.9% + $0.30)
EvntCuePlatform fee — 1% of deposit charge$25.00
EscrowHeld until finalization or cancellation$2,402.20
Schema: On deposit charge — INSERT into commission_flows two rows: one type=platform_fee amount_cents=2500, one type=stripe_processing amount_cents=7280 (estimated — actual confirmed via Stripe webhook). Update bookings.deposit_paid_at = NOW(). Update bookings.deposit_amount_cents = 250000. Stripe Payment Intent created with capture_method=manual so funds are authorized and held but not captured until finalization.

Phase 2 — Vendor/venue sets commission dial

At any point after their profile is live, vendors and venues can dial a commission percentage (0–20%) that they share with EvntCue in exchange for higher placement in Cue’s recommendations. This is a voluntary commercial decision made by the vendor. The dial is stored on their tenant profile and applied at payout — the Orgnz never sees it and it does not affect what the Orgnz pays.

Commission is calculated on the total booking value (not the deposit alone) and deducted from the vendor’s payout at escrow release. It is not charged as a separate transaction — it is withheld at payout time.
PartyActionAmount (10% dial example on $10,000 booking)
VendorDials 10% commission to EvntCue$1,000.00 withheld at payout
EvntCueReceives commission at payout$1,000.00
Vendor netReceives after commission$9,000.00 (before Stripe payout fees)
Schema: tenants.commission_dial_pct NUMERIC(4,1) DEFAULT 0 — add this field to the tenants table if not present. At payout: commission_amount_cents = ROUND(booking.amount_cents * tenant.commission_dial_pct / 100). Stored immutably on bookings.commission_amount_cents at the moment of escrow release — rate changes after that point do not affect the booking. Row inserted into commission_flows type=vndr_referral or venue_referral.

Phase 3 — Event finalized, remaining balance charged

v26.4: When the event is marked finalized (typically 7–14 days before the event date, or at a milestone agreed at booking), the remaining balance is charged to the Orgnz’s card on file. NO additional EvntCue platform fee on the balance charge — the 2.5% was earned at booking confirmation. Stripe takes their processing fee on this charge. The remainder joins escrow awaiting dual confirmation.
PartyActionAmount (remaining $7,500)
OrgnzFinal balance charged automatically$7,500.00
StripeProcessing fee — non-refundable$217.80 (2.9% + $0.30)
EvntCuePlatform fee — 1% of remaining charge$75.00
EscrowAdded to existing escrow hold$7,207.20

Phase 4 — Dual confirmation, escrow released

After the event, both the Orgnz and the vendor confirm service was delivered. When both confirmations are present, escrow is released automatically. The vendor receives their payout via Stripe Connect minus any commission dial amount. EvntCue receives the commission. If neither party confirms within 72 hours of the event end time, escrow auto-releases to the vendor (protects vendor from Orgnz ghosting).
PartyReceivesAmount (10% dial, $10,000 booking)
VendorPayout via Stripe Connect$9,000.00 (total minus 10% commission)
EvntCueCommission retained + platform fees already collected$1,000.00 commission + $100.00 platform fees = $1,100.00 total
StripeProcessing fees already deducted at charge time$290.60 total across both charges
Orgnz total paidFull booking value$10,000.00
Payout trigger logic: When bookings.orgnz_confirmed_at IS NOT NULL AND bookings.vendor_confirmed_at IS NOT NULL AND bookings.escrow_released_at IS NULL → calculate vendor_payout_cents = amount_cents - commission_amount_cents → initiate Stripe Connect transfer → set escrow_released_at = NOW(), escrow_release_method = dual_confirm → INSERT commission_flows row for commission → INSERT booking_status_log row. Atomic transaction — all steps succeed or none do.

Cancellation and refund flow

EvntCue retains a 5% cancellation fee on the total booking value regardless of when cancellation occurs. Stripe processing fees are always non-refundable (Stripe policy). Everything else is returned to the Orgnz. This must be disclosed clearly at booking confirmation — it is not a surprise fee.
ScenarioOrgnz paidEvntCue keepsStripe keepsOrgnz refund
Cancel after deposit only
($10,000 booking, $2,500 deposit paid)		$2,500.00$500.00 (5% of $10,000)$72.80 (deposit charge fee)$1,927.20
Cancel after full payment
($10,000 fully paid)		$10,000.00$500.00 (5% of $10,000)$290.60 (both charge fees)$9,209.40
Vendor-initiated cancellationWhatever paid$0 — full refund, vendor absorbsNon-refundable stillFull amount minus Stripe fees only
Edge case — small booking, deposit insufficient to cover fees: If a booking is small enough that the deposit amount is less than the 5% cancellation fee plus Stripe’s non-refundable processing fees, the Orgnz owes the difference. Example: $500 booking, $125 deposit paid. 5% cancel fee = $25. Stripe fee = $3.93. Refund = $96.07. In this case the math still works. However if deposit_pct is very low on a tiny booking, a negative refund is theoretically possible. Rule: minimum deposit must be max(25%, enough to cover 5% cancel fee + estimated Stripe fee). Enforced at booking creation: CHECK (deposit_amount_cents >= CEIL(amount_cents * 0.05) + 100) — the 100 cents buffer covers minimum Stripe fee overhead.
Vendor commission + cancellation interaction: If a booking is cancelled after the vendor had a commission dial set, the commission is not owed — it is only deducted at payout. No commission is collected on a cancelled booking. The 5% EvntCue cancellation fee is the only retention on a cancelled booking (plus Stripe’s non-refundable processing). Commission_flows rows for cancelled bookings are voided by setting status = cancelled on the booking — the commission row is never created.

Schema additions for this flow

tenants.commission_dial_pctNUMERIC(4,1) NOT NULL DEFAULT 0Vendor-set commission rate 0.0–20.0. Applied at payout. CHECK constraint: value BETWEEN 0 AND 20.
bookings.deposit_amount_centsINTEGER NOT NULL DEFAULT 0Actual deposit charged. Stored at charge time.
bookings.deposit_paid_atTIMESTAMPTZ nullableSet when deposit Stripe charge succeeds.
bookings.remaining_amount_centsINTEGER NOT NULL DEFAULT 0amount_cents minus deposit_amount_cents. Charged at finalization.
bookings.remaining_paid_atTIMESTAMPTZ nullableSet when remaining balance Stripe charge succeeds.
